Refutation of the Indivisible Part (Atom)
Author refutes atomism by demonstrating logical contradictions from the assumption of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's reductio arguments against atomism and identify the logical contradictions that force division.

Core Idea
The indivisible part is impossible because any contact forces division.

In this lesson, we explore two arguments from Hidayat al-Hikma that refute the concept of an indivisible part (atom). The first argument considers a middle part between two ends; the second examines a part placed at the meeting point of two parts. Both arguments use reductio ad absurdum to show that assuming indivisible parts leads to necessary division, thereby establishing the continuity of body.

Guided Path
1
Extract the hypothesis
Identify the assumption being refuted: the body is composed of indivisible parts (juz' la yatajaza).
This is the atomist position, common in Kalam but rejected by Peripatetic philosophers.
2
First argument: middle part and ends
Consider a middle part between two ends. Ask: does this middle part prevent the ends from meeting? If it does not, the ends would interpenetrate, contradicting the assumption of distinct parts. If it does, then the means by which it meets each end differ, so it divides.
The contradiction is that either interpenetration or division occurs, both incompatible with indivisibility.
3
Second argument: part on meeting point
Suppose a part is placed exactly on the meeting point of two other parts. Enumerate all possible ways it could contact them (ten possibilities). Show each possibility leads to division of the supposed indivisible part or of the other parts.
The enumeration is exhaustive: the part may meet none, partly, or fully each of the others, but each case implies a contradiction.
4
Conclusion from both arguments
Since both arguments lead to division, the original hypothesis of indivisible parts must be false. Body is therefore continuous and infinitely divisible.
This establishes the Peripatetic doctrine of continuous extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إبطال الجزء
الطرفين أو لا يكون، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنه لو لم يكن مانعا لكانت الأجزاء متداخلة، فلا يكون وسطا وطرفا، وقد فرضنا الوسط والطرف، وهذا خلف فثبت كونه مانعا من تلاقيها، فما به يلاقي الوسط أحد الطرفين غير ما به يلاقي الطرف الآخر، فينقسم، ولأنا لو فرضنا جزءا على ملتقى جزئين، فإما أن يلاقي واحدا منهما فقط أو ..
الأرض، والتالي باطل بالشهادة، وإن كان مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين فما به لاقى هذا الطرف غير ما به لاقى الطرف الآخر، فانقسم الجزء الوسط إلى قسمين: أحدهما
ملاق لهذا الطرف، والثاني لذلك الطرف، وكذلك الطرفان أيضا بما لاقى وبما لم يلاق. ولأنا والدليل الثاني: أنه لو كان الجسم مؤلفا من أجزاء لا تتجزى لأمكن أن يقع جزء على موضع التقاء الجزئين، فنقول: ذلك الجزء الواقع على ملتقى الجزئين إما أن لا يلاقي شيئا منهما، أو يلاقي بعض كل واحد منهما، أو يلاقي بعض واحد وكل الآخر، والأول باطل بالبداهة وإلا لم يكن على الملتقى، والثاني والثالث يستلزمان الانقسام، هذا خلف.
لو فرضنا جزءا تفصيله هكذا فإما أن يلاقي ذلك الجزء الواقع على الملتقى بتمامه أو ببعضه واحدا من المتلاقين بتمامه أو ببعضه، أو يلاقي بتمامه أو ببعضه، كلا من الجزئين بالتمام أو شيئا، أو يلاقي بتمامه أو بعضه واحدا منهما بالتمام، وبعضا من الآخر، فالاحتمالات عشرة، والأربعة الأول كلها محال بالبداهة؛ لاستلزام كل منهما أن لا يكون المفروض على الملتقى عليه، وأما الستة الآخرة كل واحد منها محال أيضا؛ إذ الأول منها يستلزم انقسام ما على ملتقى، والثاني بانقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة أجزاء، والثالث انقسام ما على الملتقى وانقسام كل من الجزئين المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والرابع انقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة وانقسام كل من الجزئين المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والخامس يقتضي انقسام ما على الملتقى وأحد المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والسادس انقسام
ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة أجزاء وأحد المتلاقيين إلى جزئين.
Chapter on Refuting the Part. The two ends or not? The second is not possible because if it were not a preventer, then the parts would interpenetrate, so there would be no middle and end, but we have assumed middle and end, which is a contradiction. So it is established that it is a preventer of their meeting. So that by which the middle meets one end is different from that by which it meets the other end; thus it divides. And also if we suppose a part on the meeting point of two parts, then either it meets only one of them or the earth, and the latter is false by testimony. And if it is a preventer of the meeting of the two ends, then that by which it met this end is different from that by which it met the other end, so the middle part divides into two parts: one meeting this end and the other that end. And likewise the two ends also by what they meet and what they do not meet. And also the second proof: if the body were composed of indivisible parts, it would be possible for a part to fall on the meeting position of two parts. So we say: that part falling on the meeting of the two parts either does not meet any of them, or meets a part of each, or meets a part of one and all of the other. The first is false by intuition, otherwise it would not be on the meeting point. The second and third necessitate division, which is a contradiction. If we suppose a part in detail like this: either that part on the meeting point meets with its entirety or with a part one of the two meeting ones with its entirety or with a part, or meets with its entirety or with a part both of the two parts entirely or something, or meets with its entirety or a part one of them entirely and a part of the other. Thus the possibilities are ten. The first four are all impossible by intuition because each necessitates that the supposed thing is not on the meeting point. As for the remaining six, each one is also impossible: the first necessitates the division of what is on the meeting point; the second the division of what is on the meeting point into three parts; the third the division of what is on the meeting point and the division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fourth the division of what is on the meeting point into three and the division of each of the two meeting parts into two; the fifth requires the division of what is on the meeting point and one of the two meeting ones into two parts; the sixth the division of what is on the meeting point into three parts and one of the two meeting ones into two. Therefore division necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the concept of an indivisible part (atom). The author employs two main reductio ad absurdum arguments. First, he considers a middle part between two ends: if it is not a preventer, the parts would interpenetrate; if it is a preventer, then the means of contact with each end differs, forcing division. Second, he examines a part placed at the meeting point of two parts. He enumerates ten hypothetical ways this part could contact the others, and shows each leads to an impossibility, thus proving that parts must be divisible. The argument relies on intuitive impossibility (badaha) and contradiction (khalaf). This refutation is foundational for establishing the continuous nature of body.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزى (juz' la yatajaza): An indivisible part, the atom that the author seeks to refute. [Note: In Kalam, atom is a standard concept; here it is being refuted from a Peripatetic perspective.]
ملتقى (multaqa): The meeting point or contact point of two parts.
مانع (mani'): A preventer that stops the two ends from meeting directly, causing the middle part to be distinct.
انقسام (inqisam): Division, the necessary outcome counter to the hypothesis of indivisibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second argument, what is the consequence if a part on the meeting point meets a part of one and all of the other?
A) It remains indivisible.
B) It necessitates division.
C) It shows the parts are separate.
D) It proves atoms exist.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of indivisible parts leads to necessary division, proving that body is continuous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the first argument (middle part) as a formal reductio: assume a body of indivisible parts, then consider a middle part and derive a contradiction.
?
For the second argument, take the third possibility (a part on the meeting point meets a part of one and all of the other) and show step-by-step why it necessitates division.

Critique of the Peripatetic Two-Extension Doctrine
Author critiques the Peripatetic two-extension theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Peripatetic argument for two extensions and identify the author's logical objection based on the impossibility of interpenetration.

Core Idea
If two extensions can unite, two bodies can interpenetrate — an absurdity.

The Peripatetics held that bodies contain a substantial extension that persists and an accidental extension that changes. The author argues this leads to a logical contradiction: if two extensions can coexist in the same location, then two entire bodies could occupy the same space. Let us follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Peripatetic claim
Find where the Peripatetics assert that a body has two extensions: substantial and accidental. Note the distinction: substantial remains during rarefaction/condensation, accidental changes.
The passage states: 'وقالوا: في الجسم امتدادان جوهري وعرضي'
2
Extract the difference between the extensions
The substantial extension persists, the accidental extension changes. This implies they are distinct entities occupying the same body.
3
Understand the author's objection
The author argues that if two distinct extensions can coexist in the same position and volume, then why cannot two entire bodies interpenetrate? The impossibility of interpenetration is due to the nature of extension. Allowing two extensions violates this principle.
Read: 'ولو جاز ذلك لجاز تداخل الجسمين'
4
Formalize the logical consequence
If the antecedent (two extensions unite) is possible, then the consequent (interpenetration of two bodies) is also possible. Since the consequent is false (interpenetration impossible), the antecedent must be false. This is a modus tollens argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل جسم إلخ هذا عند المشائين، وأما عند الإشراقيين: فالجوهر المتصل قائم بذاته غير حال في شيء آخر، وهو عين حقيقة الجسم. يحل الحلول في اللغة: فرود آمدن چیزی در چیزی، وفي الاصطلاح الاختصاص الناعت أي يكون لوجود الشيء تعلق بشيء آخر بحيث لا يتصور وجوده بدون ذلك الشيء، ويكون نعتا له وذلك منعوتا به، فإن كان ذلك الشيء ذا وضع يكونان متحدين فيه. الحال: هو الصورة الجسمية: معناها عندهم جوهر ممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة، وقالوا: في الجسم امتدادان جوهري وعرضي، الأول هو جزء الجسم مقوم للهيولى، والثاني: كم سار فيه وأثبتوا التغاير بينهما أن الأول يبقى عند التخلخل والتكاثف، والثاني يتبدل ويتغير والمتبدل غير الباقي أقول: إن القول بالامتدادين في غاية العسر لا يشهد عليه بداهة العقل بل تأبى الفطرة السليمة حتى الشيخ شهاب الدين ذكر أن بعض الأجسام القول مبهما، وقال: إن في الجسم امتدادا واحدا لا غير، وغاية ما قالوا أن الامتداد الجوهري مبهم التقدر لا يمسح بكذا وكذا، ولا يقدر أنه كذا، ولا يوصف بحسب ذلك بالمساواة والكلية والجزئية؛ لأن كل ذلك بحسب التقدر، وهو في مرتبة ماهية معرى عن ذلك كله، وإنما تلحقه تلك الأحكام بحسب عروض الكم الساري فيه، ولا يخفى عليك أن القول بالامتدادين بحيث يتحدان في الوضع والحجم لا يقبله العقل السليم، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز تداخل الجسمين؛ لأن امتناع التداخل فيه إنما هو بحسب طبيعة الامتداد، ولما جاز اتحاد الامتدادين فبأي أمر منع العقل التداخل فيه. بعض الأجسام المراد ببعض الأجسام الجسم المفرد، وهو الذي لا يكون مركبا من أجسام لا المركب، وهو ما يكون مركبا من أجسام؛ لأن الاتصال الوحداني لم يثبت في المركبات؛ لبقاء الصور النوعية للبسائط، ومع بقائها لا ينافي الاتصال الحقيقي. نعم، فيها اتصال حسي والكلام ههنا في الحقيقي. والقبول قد يطلق ويراد به الاستعداد أعني عدم الشيء عن محل قابل له وهذا المعنى مقابل للفعل، وقد يراد به مطلق الاتصاف وهذا لا ينافي الفعلية، والظاهر أن المراد به ههنا هذا المعنى الثاني أي مطلق الاتصاف يعني إذا طرأ الانفكاك على الجسم المفرد يجب أن يكون هو في نفسه قبل الانفكاك متصلا واحدا كما هو عند الحس، ولو كان المراد به الأول - أعني يلغو الحكم عليه بأنه يجب أن يكون متصلا؛ لأن استعداد الشيء عبارة عن عدمه عن محل قابل له فلا بد أن يكون الانفكاك فيه بالفعل معدوما، ثم يحصل بالقسمة، وإذا كان الانفكاك معدوما كان متصلا فيكون قوله: "بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك" في قوة "الأجسام المتصلة"، وهذا - كما ترى – ليس بمفيد إلا أن يقال: المراد: بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك بحسب الحس، فحينئذ يصح حمل القبول على المعنى الأول - أعني الاستعداد بحسب الظاهر؛ لأن الانفصال الطارئ لم يكن بحسب الظاهر من قبل، فيكون بالقوة.
Every body, etc.: This is according to the Peripatetics. As for the Ishraqiyun, the continuous substance is self-subsistent, not residing in another, and it is the very reality of the body. 'Hulul' (indwelling) in language means the coming of something into something; in technical terminology, it is the attributive specification, i.e., the existence of a thing has a connection to another thing such that its existence is not conceived without that thing, and it is a predicate for it and that is the subject of the predicate. If that thing has position, they are united in it. The resident (al-hal) is the corporeal form; its meaning according to them is a substance extended in three dimensions. And they said: In a body there are two extensions: substantial and accidental. The first is the part of the body constituting the hyle, the second is quantity flowing in it. They established the difference between them that the first remains during rarefaction and condensation, while the second changes and alters, and what changes is other than what remains. I say: The claim of two extensions is extremely difficult; sound intellect does not testify to it, rather sound nature rejects it, until Shaykh Shihab al-Din mentioned that 'some bodies'—the statement is ambiguous—and said: In a body there is only one extension, no other. The utmost they said is that the substantial extension is indetermined in measure; it is not measured by such-and-such, nor determined as such, nor described thereby by equality, totality, or partiality, because all that is according to determination, and it is at the level of quiddity stripped of all that; rather those judgments attach to it by virtue of the accidental quantity flowing in it. It is not hidden from you that the claim of two extensions such that they are united in position and volume is not accepted by sound intellect, and if that were permissible, then the interpenetration of two bodies would be permissible, because the impossibility of interpenetration is by virtue of the nature of extension. And since the union of two extensions is permitted, by what means does intellect prevent interpenetration? 'Some bodies' means the simple body, which is not composed of bodies, not the composite, which is composed of bodies, because unitary continuity is not established in composites due to the persistence of the specific forms of simples, and with their persistence, real continuity is not negated. Yes, there is sensory continuity in them, but the discussion here is about real continuity. And 'acceptance' (qabul) may be used and meant as 'potentiality' (isti'dad), meaning the absence of a thing from a substrate capable of it, and this meaning is opposite to actuality; and it may be used and meant as absolute attribution (mutlaq al-ittisaf), which does not negate actuality. The apparent meaning here is the second meaning, i.e., absolute attribution, meaning that when separation occurs on the simple body, it must have been in itself before separation continuous and one as it is in sensation. And if the first meaning were intended, the judgment that it must be continuous would be invalid, because the potentiality of a thing is its absence from a capable substrate, so separation must actually be nonexistent in it, then division occurs, and if separation is nonexistent, it is continuous—so his statement 'some bodies capable of separation' is equivalent to 'connected bodies'. And this, as you see, is not useful unless it is said that what is meant is 'some bodies capable of separation according to sensation'; then it becomes correct to take 'acceptance' in the first meaning, i.e., potentiality according to appearance, because the accidental separation was not present in appearance before, so it is in potentiality.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage critically examines two related metaphysical doctrines: the Peripatetic theory of two extensions in a body and the interpretation of 'some bodies capable of separation'. The author begins by contrasting the Peripatetic view with that of the Ishraqiyun (Illuminationists), who hold that the continuous substance is self-subsistent and identical to the body's reality—a direct rejection of the hylomorphic composition. The author then clarifies the technical meaning of 'hulul' (indwelling) as an attributive relation where one thing's existence depends on another. He defines 'al-hal' as the corporeal form, a substance extended in three dimensions. The Peripatetics claim two extensions: substantial (constitutive of hyle) and accidental (quantity flowing in it), distinguishing them by the substantial extension's persistence during rarefaction/condensation. The author strongly critiques this, stating that sound reason rejects such a union of two extensions in the same position and volume, as it would imply the possibility of two bodies interpenetrating. He then turns to the expression 'some bodies' in the original text, explaining that it refers to a simple body (not composed of other bodies), and clarifies that unitary continuity only applies to such bodies, not composites with specific forms. Finally, he debates the meaning of 'qabul' (acceptance): whether it signifies potentiality (absence of a quality in a substrate) or absolute attribution. The apparent meaning in context is absolute attribution: the body before separation is continuous in itself. If potentiality were meant, the statement would be circular. However, if 'capable of separation' is taken as 'capable according to sensation', then potentiality is appropriate because the actual separation was not apparent before.

Key Terms
امتداد جوهري
امتداد عرضي
تداخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does the author derive from the Peripatetic two-extension doctrine?
A) Bodies would be indivisible.
B) Two bodies could interpenetrate.
C) Substantial extension is eternal.
D) Accidental extension is illusory.

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic theory of two extensions leads to the absurd consequence that two bodies could occupy the same space, so sound reason rejects it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar argument: If a body could be both black and white simultaneously, then opposites could coexist. Use the author's method (reductio ad absurdum) to show this is impossible. Hint: start from the premise that two properties can coexist in the same subject, then derive an absurd consequence.

Simple vs. Composite Bodies and Real Continuity
Author explains the meaning of 'some bodies' as simple bodies and clarifies continuity types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple and composite bodies and explain why real continuity applies only to simple bodies.

Core Idea
Real continuity exists only in simple bodies; composites have only sensory continuity.

The passage clarifies that 'some bodies' refers to simple bodies. The author explains that unitary continuity (ittiṣāl wuḥdānī) is not found in composites because each simple part retains its specific form. Let's unpack this distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of bodies
Find where the passage defines 'some bodies' as 'al-jism al-mufrad' (simple body) and contrasts it with 'al-murakkab' (composite).
The passage says: 'بعض الأجسام المراد ببعض الأجسام الجسم المفرد، وهو الذي لا يكون مركبا من أجسام لا المركب'
2
Understand why composites lack real continuity
Composites are made of simples each retaining their specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya). Because these forms persist, the parts remain distinct, preventing true unity.
The passage mentions 'بقاء الصور النوعية للبسائط'
3
Distinguish real from sensory continuity
Real continuity (ittiṣāl ḥaqīqī) is a metaphysical unity; sensory continuity (ittiṣāl ḥissī) is mere appearance. The author emphasizes that the discussion here concerns real continuity.
نعم، فيها اتصال حسي والكلام ههنا في الحقيقي
4
Apply the distinction
Recognize that when the original text says 'some bodies capable of separation', it refers to simple bodies. Thus, they have real continuity before separation.

كل جسم إلخ هذا عند المشائين، وأما عند الإشراقيين: فالجوهر المتصل قائم بذاته غير حال في شيء آخر، وهو عين حقيقة الجسم. يحل الحلول في اللغة: فرود آمدن چیزی در چیزی، وفي الاصطلاح الاختصاص الناعت أي يكون لوجود الشيء تعلق بشيء آخر بحيث لا يتصور وجوده بدون ذلك الشيء، ويكون نعتا له وذلك منعوتا به، فإن كان ذلك الشيء ذا وضع يكونان متحدين فيه. الحال: هو الصورة الجسمية: معناها عندهم جوهر ممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة، وقالوا: في الجسم امتدادان جوهري وعرضي، الأول هو جزء الجسم مقوم للهيولى، والثاني: كم سار فيه وأثبتوا التغاير بينهما أن الأول يبقى عند التخلخل والتكاثف، والثاني يتبدل ويتغير والمتبدل غير الباقي أقول: إن القول بالامتدادين في غاية العسر لا يشهد عليه بداهة العقل بل تأبى الفطرة السليمة حتى الشيخ شهاب الدين ذكر أن بعض الأجسام القول مبهما، وقال: إن في الجسم امتدادا واحدا لا غير، وغاية ما قالوا أن الامتداد الجوهري مبهم التقدر لا يمسح بكذا وكذا، ولا يقدر أنه كذا، ولا يوصف بحسب ذلك بالمساواة والكلية والجزئية؛ لأن كل ذلك بحسب التقدر، وهو في مرتبة ماهية معرى عن ذلك كله، وإنما تلحقه تلك الأحكام بحسب عروض الكم الساري فيه، ولا يخفى عليك أن القول بالامتدادين بحيث يتحدان في الوضع والحجم لا يقبله العقل السليم، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز تداخل الجسمين؛ لأن امتناع التداخل فيه إنما هو بحسب طبيعة الامتداد، ولما جاز اتحاد الامتدادين فبأي أمر منع العقل التداخل فيه. بعض الأجسام المراد ببعض الأجسام الجسم المفرد، وهو الذي لا يكون مركبا من أجسام لا المركب، وهو ما يكون مركبا من أجسام؛ لأن الاتصال الوحداني لم يثبت في المركبات؛ لبقاء الصور النوعية للبسائط، ومع بقائها لا ينافي الاتصال الحقيقي. نعم، فيها اتصال حسي والكلام ههنا في الحقيقي. والقبول قد يطلق ويراد به الاستعداد أعني عدم الشيء عن محل قابل له وهذا المعنى مقابل للفعل، وقد يراد به مطلق الاتصاف وهذا لا ينافي الفعلية، والظاهر أن المراد به ههنا هذا المعنى الثاني أي مطلق الاتصاف يعني إذا طرأ الانفكاك على الجسم المفرد يجب أن يكون هو في نفسه قبل الانفكاك متصلا واحدا كما هو عند الحس، ولو كان المراد به الأول - أعني يلغو الحكم عليه بأنه يجب أن يكون متصلا؛ لأن استعداد الشيء عبارة عن عدمه عن محل قابل له فلا بد أن يكون الانفكاك فيه بالفعل معدوما، ثم يحصل بالقسمة، وإذا كان الانفكاك معدوما كان متصلا فيكون قوله: "بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك" في قوة "الأجسام المتصلة"، وهذا - كما ترى – ليس بمفيد إلا أن يقال: المراد: بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك بحسب الحس، فحينئذ يصح حمل القبول على المعنى الأول - أعني الاستعداد بحسب الظاهر؛ لأن الانفصال الطارئ لم يكن بحسب الظاهر من قبل، فيكون بالقوة.
Every body, etc.: This is according to the Peripatetics. As for the Ishraqiyun, the continuous substance is self-subsistent, not residing in another, and it is the very reality of the body. 'Hulul' (indwelling) in language means the coming of something into something; in technical terminology, it is the attributive specification, i.e., the existence of a thing has a connection to another thing such that its existence is not conceived without that thing, and it is a predicate for it and that is the subject of the predicate. If that thing has position, they are united in it. The resident (al-hal) is the corporeal form; its meaning according to them is a substance extended in three dimensions. And they said: In a body there are two extensions: substantial and accidental. The first is the part of the body constituting the hyle, the second is quantity flowing in it. They established the difference between them that the first remains during rarefaction and condensation, while the second changes and alters, and what changes is other than what remains. I say: The claim of two extensions is extremely difficult; sound intellect does not testify to it, rather sound nature rejects it, until Shaykh Shihab al-Din mentioned that 'some bodies'—the statement is ambiguous—and said: In a body there is only one extension, no other. The utmost they said is that the substantial extension is indetermined in measure; it is not measured by such-and-such, nor determined as such, nor described thereby by equality, totality, or partiality, because all that is according to determination, and it is at the level of quiddity stripped of all that; rather those judgments attach to it by virtue of the accidental quantity flowing in it. It is not hidden from you that the claim of two extensions such that they are united in position and volume is not accepted by sound intellect, and if that were permissible, then the interpenetration of two bodies would be permissible, because the impossibility of interpenetration is by virtue of the nature of extension. And since the union of two extensions is permitted, by what means does intellect prevent interpenetration? 'Some bodies' means the simple body, which is not composed of bodies, not the composite, which is composed of bodies, because unitary continuity is not established in composites due to the persistence of the specific forms of simples, and with their persistence, real continuity is not negated. Yes, there is sensory continuity in them, but the discussion here is about real continuity. And 'acceptance' (qabul) may be used and meant as 'potentiality' (isti'dad), meaning the absence of a thing from a substrate capable of it, and this meaning is opposite to actuality; and it may be used and meant as absolute attribution (mutlaq al-ittisaf), which does not negate actuality. The apparent meaning here is the second meaning, i.e., absolute attribution, meaning that when separation occurs on the simple body, it must have been in itself before separation continuous and one as it is in sensation. And if the first meaning were intended, the judgment that it must be continuous would be invalid, because the potentiality of a thing is its absence from a capable substrate, so separation must actually be nonexistent in it, then division occurs, and if separation is nonexistent, it is continuous—so his statement 'some bodies capable of separation' is equivalent to 'connected bodies'. And this, as you see, is not useful unless it is said that what is meant is 'some bodies capable of separation according to sensation'; then it becomes correct to take 'acceptance' in the first meaning, i.e., potentiality according to appearance, because the accidental separation was not present in appearance before, so it is in potentiality.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage critically examines two related metaphysical doctrines: the Peripatetic theory of two extensions in a body and the interpretation of 'some bodies capable of separation'. The author begins by contrasting the Peripatetic view with that of the Ishraqiyun (Illuminationists), who hold that the continuous substance is self-subsistent and identical to the body's reality—a direct rejection of the hylomorphic composition. The author then clarifies the technical meaning of 'hulul' (indwelling) as an attributive relation where one thing's existence depends on another. He defines 'al-hal' as the corporeal form, a substance extended in three dimensions. The Peripatetics claim two extensions: substantial (constitutive of hyle) and accidental (quantity flowing in it), distinguishing them by the substantial extension's persistence during rarefaction/condensation. The author strongly critiques this, stating that sound reason rejects such a union of two extensions in the same position and volume, as it would imply the possibility of two bodies interpenetrating. He then turns to the expression 'some bodies' in the original text, explaining that it refers to a simple body (not composed of other bodies), and clarifies that unitary continuity only applies to such bodies, not composites with specific forms. Finally, he debates the meaning of 'qabul' (acceptance): whether it signifies potentiality (absence of a quality in a substrate) or absolute attribution. The apparent meaning in context is absolute attribution: the body before separation is continuous in itself. If potentiality were meant, the statement would be circular. However, if 'capable of separation' is taken as 'capable according to sensation', then potentiality is appropriate because the actual separation was not apparent before.

Key Terms
جسم مفرد
مركب
اتصال حقيقي
اتصال حسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say composites lack real continuity?
A) Because they are too large.
B) Because the specific forms of their parts persist and prevent unity.
C) Because they are made of atoms.
D) Because they can be divided.

Key Takeaway
Real continuity is a property of simple bodies only; composites only appear continuous to the senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example of a composite body (e.g., water in a glass) and explain why, according to the author, it does not have real continuity. Then provide an example of a simple body (if it exists according to the author) and explain why it does.

Resolving the Ambiguity of 'Qabul': Potentiality vs. Attribution
Author resolves the ambiguity of 'qabul' in the phrase 'some bodies capable of separation'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between two meanings of 'qabul' (potentiality and absolute attribution) and determine which meaning fits the context of the passage.

Core Idea
The term 'qabul' is ambiguous; the correct meaning here is absolute attribution, not potentiality.

The author examines the phrase 'some bodies capable of separation'. The key term is 'qabul' (acceptance). He shows that if it means potentiality, the statement becomes circular; if it means absolute attribution, it correctly describes the body's continuous state before separation. Let's see how he arrives at this resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of qabul
Find where the passage defines qabul as 'isti'dad' (potentiality) and as 'mutlaq al-ittisaf' (absolute attribution). Note that potentiality implies absence, while attribution does not.
The passage says: 'القبول قد يطلق ويراد به الاستعداد... وقد يراد به مطلق الاتصاف'
2
Apply the first meaning (potentiality)
If qabul means potentiality, then 'some bodies capable of separation' would mean 'some bodies in which separation is absent (potential)'. Since separation is absent, the body is already continuous, so the statement becomes trivial.
The author says this would be equivalent to saying 'connected bodies'.
3
Apply the second meaning (absolute attribution)
If qabul means absolute attribution, then 'capable of separation' simply describes the body as being the kind of thing that can be separated, without implying that separation is currently absent. This preserves the intended meaning.
The author prefers this meaning: 'الظاهر أن المراد به ههنا هذا المعنى الثاني'
4
Consider the exception
The author notes that if 'capable of separation' is qualified by 'according to sensation' (ḥiss), then potentiality works, because the actual separation was not apparent before.
This is a compromise to salvage the potentiality interpretation.

According to the author, what problem arises if 'qabul' is interpreted as potentiality in 'some bodies capable of separation'?

The Syllogistic Proof That All Bodies Are Composed of Matter and Form
Author extends hylomorphic composition from one body to all bodies via the dependence of corporeal form on matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves every body is composed of hylē and form, and explain how the author resolves the objection about form's independence.

Core Idea
Every body must have matter because corporeal form cannot exist independently as an individual.

In the previous lesson, you saw the proof that a single body is composite. Now the author extends this to all bodies using a universal categorical syllogism. The key move is to show that the corporeal form (al-ṭabīʿa al-miqdāriyya) cannot be independent of a locus; otherwise, it would not inhere in matter, contradicting the earlier proof. An objection about form not needing matter for its essence is resolved by a distinction between essential and individual existence. This lesson walks you through that syllogistic reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The author wants to prove that every body (jism) is composed of hylē (matter) and form (sūra). Locate the statement: 'وجب أن تكون الأجسام كلها مركبة من الهيولى والصورة'.
This is the universal claim to be demonstrated.
2
Find the major premise
The major premise concerns the quantifiable nature (corporeal form). It says: either it is independent of a locus or not. The first alternative (independence) is impossible. The impossibility is argued: if it were independent, it could not inhere in matter, but inherence has been proven.
This is a disjunctive premise that sets up the choice.
3
Extract the minor premise
The minor premise is the assertion that the first alternative (independence) is false. So the second alternative (dependence on a locus) must be true. The author states: 'والأول محال' (the first is impossible). Thus the corporeal form necessarily depends on a locus (matter).
This follows from the proven inherence of form in matter.
4
Recognize the syllogistic structure
The argument is a categorical syllogism in the first figure: Major: Everything that is not independent of a locus requires matter. Minor: The corporeal form is not independent of a locus. Conclusion: Therefore, the corporeal form (and hence every body having it) is composed of matter. The author then notes the condition of specific vs. generic nature to address universality.
If corporeal nature were generic, different species might have different requirements, but here it is taken as specific.
5
Resolve the objection with a distinction
An objector says: form does not need matter for its existence; rather matter needs form. The author replies: dependence here means at the level of absolute individuality (al-fardiyya al-muṭlaqa), not essence (māhiyya). Thus form can be independent in its quiddity but dependent for concrete instantiation.
This avoids contradiction by distinguishing two orders: essence vs. individual existence.

وإذا ثبت أن ذلك الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة وجب أن تكون الأجسام كلها مركبة من الهيولى والصورة؛ لأن الطبيعة المقدارية إما أن تكون بذاتها غنية عن المحل أو لم تكن، والأول محال كما بذاتها وهذا الحكم صحيح على تقدير كون الطبيعة الجسمية حقيقة نوعية؛ لأن مقتضى الطبيعة النوعية لا يختلف في أفرادها، وأما على تقدير كونها طبيعة جنسية فلا؛ إذ يجوز أن يختلف مقتضاها في أفرادها بحسب الفصول المنوعة، وقد يدعى أن كون الطبيعة المقدارية إما أن تكون بذاتها غنية عن المحل أو لم تكن، والأول محال علاقة ما، وأما علاقة الحلول فليست ببينة، ولعل القريحة السليمة تحكم بأنه إذا كان كل واحد من الأمرين متباينا منفصلا ليس أحدهما حالا في الآخر استغنى كل منهما من الآخر، فلا يكون بينهما توحد طبعي يمنع من الانفكاك، فيكون الحقيقة اعتبارية لا محصلة، فافهم. إذا ثبت إلخ يعني إذا ثبت أن ذلك الجسم المفرد مركب من الهيولى والصورة كان كذلك، كل جسم يعني مؤلفا من الهيولى والصورة؛ لأن الصورة الجسمية لا تخلو إما أن تكون غنية بذاتها عن محل تقوم به أو لا، والأول باطل؛ لأنها لو كانت غنية بذاتها عن المحل لما أمكن حلولها في المحل؛ لأن حلول الشيء في الشيء يستلزم الافتقار إليه كما دريت من قبل أن الحلول هو وجود الشيء في الشيء بحيث لا يمكن بدون ذلك، والغناء ينافيه والتالي باطل؛ لأن حلول الصورة الجسمية في الهيولى قد ثبت من قبل بالبرهان فتعين الشق الثاني أي افتقارها بذاتها إلى الهيولى، فلا يخلو فرد من الصورة الجسمية من الهيولى فتبين أن كل جسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. فإن قيل: إن الصورة الجسمية ليست محتاجة في وجودها إلى الهيولى، بل الأمر بالعكس، سيجيء في بحث التلازم بين الهيولى والصورة أن الهيولى محتاجة إلى الصورة في وجودها وبقائها والصورة ليست محتاجة إليها في وجودها وإلا لزم الدور، بل في تشخصها وتشكلها وقد صرحوا به قلت مراد القوم من الافتقار الذاتي ههنا الافتقار بحسب الفردية المطلقة لا بحسب الماهية، ولا يلزم التناقض بين الكلامين؛ لأن الافتقار في مرتبة الفردية، والغناء في مرتبة الماهية نعم لو كان الغناء والافتقار في مرتبة واحدة للزم ذلك.
And if it is established that that body is composed of hylē and form, it must be that all bodies are composed of hylē and form; because the quantifiable nature either is in itself independent of a locus or not. The first is impossible. As for itself, this judgment is correct on the supposition that the corporeal nature is a specific reality; because the requirement of a specific nature does not differ among its individuals. But on the supposition that it is a generic nature, then not; because its requirement may differ among its individuals according to specific differences. And it may be claimed that the quantifiable nature either is in itself independent of a locus or not, and the first is impossible. A relation, and the relation of inherence is not evident. Perhaps the sound intellect judges that if each of the two is separate and distinct, not one inhering in the other, each is independent of the other, so there is no natural unity preventing separation, and the reality would be conceptual not realized. So understand. 'If it is established' etc., means if it is established that that single body is composed of hylē and form, then likewise every body means composed of hylē and form; because the corporeal form is either in itself independent of a locus that supports it or not. The first is false; because if it were independent of the locus, its inherence in the locus would be impossible; since the inherence of something in something entails its dependence on it, as you learned before: inherence is the existence of something in something such that it cannot be without it. Independence contradicts that. And the consequent is false because the inherence of corporeal form in hylē has been proven earlier by demonstration. So the second alternative is determined, i.e., its essential dependence on hylē. Hence no individual of corporeal form is free of hylē, so it becomes clear that every body is composed of hylē and form. If it is said: The corporeal form does not need hylē for its existence, rather the opposite; it will come in the discussion of the interdependence between hylē and form that hylē needs form for its existence and persistence, and form does not need hylē for its existence—otherwise circularity would occur—rather it needs it for its individuation and configuration, and they have stated that. I say: The intended meaning of the people regarding essential dependence here is dependence with respect to absolute individuality, not with respect to essence. There is no contradiction between the two statements because dependence is at the level of individuality and independence is at the level of essence. Indeed, if independence and dependence were at the same level, contradiction would follow.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the proof for hylomorphism, extending it from one body to all bodies. The author first asserts that if one body is composite, then all bodies must be, because the quantifiable nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-miqdāriyya) either is independent of a locus or not. The impossibility of independence is argued by noting that if corporeal form were independent, it could not inhere in matter—yet its inherence has been proven. Thus, every corporeal form depends on matter. An objection is raised: form does not depend on matter for existence; rather matter depends on form. The author resolves this by distinguishing levels: at the level of essence (māhiyya), form is independent, but at the level of individual instances (fardiyya), it depends on matter for instantiation. This avoids contradiction. The author also discusses the nature of corporeal nature as specific or generic, affecting the universality of the claim. The passage reinforces the necessity of matter-form composition in all bodies through rigorous dialectical reasoning.

الحلول
الافتقار الذاتي
الفردية المطلقة

Corporeal Form and Receptivity to Separation
Author demonstrates corporeal form's receptivity to separation and answers an objection about shape change without separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad infinitum argument for corporeal form's receptivity to separation and identify the commentator's correction that avoids infinite regress.

Core Idea
Separability of form leads to infinite regress unless corrected by 'association'.

In this passage from Hidayat al-Hikma, the author argues that corporeal form must be receptive to separation, using a reductio. However, this leads to an infinite regress if we claim the form is composed of matter and form. The commentator corrects this by saying the form is merely 'associated with matter.' An objection about a candle's shape change is then answered, showing that even apparent continuous changes involve separation at a microscopic level.

Guided Path
1
Extract the main claim and its proof
Identify the claim that corporeal form is receptive to separation. The proof is a reductio: if it were not receptive, shape change would be impossible, but shape change occurs, so it must be receptive.
Look for the phrase 'وإلا لأمكن زواله' indicating the conditional.
2
Identify the syllogism that generates infinite regress
The author states: 'Everything receptive to separation is composed of matter and form.' Combined with the claim that corporeal form is receptive, we conclude that form is composed of matter and form, which itself is receptive, leading to an infinite regress.
The syllogism is in Barbara mood of the first figure.
3
Understand the commentator's correction
To avoid infinite regress, the commentator replaces 'composed of' with 'associated with'. Now: 'Everything receptive to separation is associated with matter.' This blocks the regress because association does not entail composition.
The correction preserves the connection between form and matter without the problematic composition.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objector says a candle changes shape without separation (e.g., melting). The response argues that even in a candle, parts connect and separate microscopically, so separation does occur. The commentator suggests using 'receptivity to affection' instead of 'separation' to avoid the objection entirely.
This shows the importance of precise terminology in philosophical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الصورة الجسمية قابلة للانفصال. وإلا لأمكن زواله، فأمكن أن تتشكل الصورة بشكل آخر، فتكون قابلة للانفصال، وكل ما يقبل الانفصال فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة، فالصورة مركبة من الهيولى والصورة، وتلك الصورة أيضا قابلة للانفصال، فتكون تلك أيضا مركبة منهما، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، وأيضا يلزم تركب الشيء من نفسه ومن غيره، وبطلانه لا يخفى على أحد. فالصواب أن يقول: كل ما يقبل الانفصال فهو مقارن للهيولى. فإن قيل: قد يتبدل الشكل من غير ورود الانفصال كما في الشمعة فلا يصح قوله: قابلة للانفصال. قلت: أجيب عنه: أن تبدل أشكال الشمعة أيضا لا يخلو عن اتصال أجزاء كانت متفرقة، وافتراق أجزاء كانت متصلة، فيلزم الفصل. ولو قال بدل الانفصال الانفعال، كان الأمر أسهل و لم يتوجه هذا النقص.
Section on that corporeal form is receptive to separation. And if not, then its removal would be possible, so it is possible for the form to take on another shape, thus it is receptive to separation. And everything receptive to separation is composed of matter and form, so the form is composed of matter and form, and that form is also receptive to separation, so that also is composed of them, and so on ad infinitum. Also it entails composition of a thing from itself and from another, whose falsity is obvious. So the correct thing is to say: 'Everything receptive to separation is associated with matter.' For if it is said: Shape may change without the occurrence of separation, as in a candle, so his statement 'receptive to separation' is not valid. I say: The answer to it is that the change of the candle's shapes also does not lack connection of parts that were separate and separation of parts that were connected, so separation occurs. And if he had said 'receptivity to affection' instead of separation, the matter would be easier and this criticism would not arise.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument establishing that corporeal form (الصورة الجسمية) is receptive to separation (الانفصال). The author begins by stating the claim in the section title. The argument proceeds by reductio: if the form were not receptive to separation, then its shape could never change. But shapes do change, therefore it must be receptive. From this receptivity, the author then argues that everything receptive to separation is composed of matter (هيولى) and form. This leads to an infinite regress because the corporeal form itself would be composed of matter and form, and that form would also be receptive, and so on, also implying composition of a thing from itself and another. To avoid this absurdity, the commentator corrects the original text: instead of saying 'composed of matter and form,' it should say 'associated with matter.' Then an objection is raised: shape can change without separation, as in a candle that changes shape when melted. The response is that even in a candle, the change involves connection and separation of parts, so separation still occurs. The commentator suggests that if the original author had used the term 'receptivity to affection' (انفعال) instead of 'separation,' the objection would not have arisen. The passage thus refines the concept and defends it against a natural objection.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
الانفصال
الهيولى

B) Because matter and form cannot be composed
C) Because the form is not actually composed
D) Because association is a weaker relation

Key Takeaway
The key logical move is replacing composition with association to block infinite regress while preserving the receptivity of form to separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Disjunctive Syllogism for Matter's Inseparability from Form
Author proves matter must always be associated with form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disjunctive syllogism used in the proof that matter cannot be stripped of form and verify its validity.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism eliminates both options, proving matter must always have form.

The author presents a rigorous proof that matter (al-hayūlā) is never found without form. The proof takes the form of a conditional argument: if matter could exist without form, then it would either have spatial position or not. Both alternatives are shown to be impossible, so matter must always be associated with form. This lesson walks you through the logical structure of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion being proved
Find the final claim: matter cannot be stripped of form.
2
Extract the disjunctive premise
The author assumes the opposite: if matter were stripped of form, it would either have position or not. This is the disjunction.
3
Analyze the first branch: having position
If matter has position, it must be divisible (since everything with position is divisible). But matter in itself is not divisible. So this branch is impossible.
The indivisibility of matter is established earlier in the text.
4
Analyze the second branch: not having position
The author claims this is also impossible, though the text does not elaborate directly. The impossibility is derived from the nature of matter as a substance.
Matter is defined as a substance receptive to form, and substance requires position in some sense.
5
Combine the branches
Both alternatives are impossible, so the original assumption (matter stripped of form) is false. Therefore, matter cannot exist without form.
This is a valid disjunctive syllogism (modus tollendo ponens with negation of both disjuncts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الهيولى لا تتجرد عن الصورة. لأنها لو تجردت عن الصورة، فإما أن تكون ذات وضع أو لا تكون. لا سبيل إلى كل واحد من القسمين، فلا سبيل إلى تجردها عن الصورة. أما أنه لا سبيل إلى الأول فلأنها حينئذ إما أن تنقسم أو لا، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن كل ما له وضع فهو منقسم على ما مر في نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزى. الحق أنه لا حاجة إلى إبطال كون الهيولى ذات وضع، وترديدها إلى جزء لا يتجزى وخط جوهري وسطح جوهري وجسم؛ لأن الكلام ههنا في الهيولى التي تبين حقيقتها من قبل من أنها جوهر قابل للصورة وليست في حد ذاتها متصلة ولا منفصلة ولا واحدة ولا متعددة ولا متحيزة، بل إنما تلحقها تلك الأحكام من قبل الصورة. ومن البين أنها إذ ليست في حد ذاتها ذات وضع لا تجري تلك الاحتمالات. والتلازم إنما يثبتون بين الصورة والهيولى التي أثبتوها بالبرهان، وهي ليست ذات وضع، فأية حاجة إلى إبطال كونها ذات وضع. وأما الهيولى بالمعنى الآخر فليس الكلام ههنا فيه، وإنه بمعزل عن المقصود بمراحل كما لا يخفى. لأنه قيل: إن النقطة ذات وضع وليست بمنقسمة، أجيب عنه: أن المراد به كل ما له وضع بالاستقلال لا بالتبع. وحاصله كل جوهر ذي وضع فهو منقسم؛ بناء على ما مر من بطلان الجزء الذي لا يتجزى.
Section on that matter does not become stripped of form. Because if it were stripped of form, then it would either have position or not. Neither of the two divisions is possible, so it cannot be stripped of form. As for the first being impossible, because then it would either be divisible or not. The second is impossible because everything that has position is divisible, as was mentioned in the refutation of the indivisible part. The truth is that there is no need to refute that matter has position, and to enumerate it into indivisible part, substantial line, substantial surface, and body; because the discussion here is about matter whose reality has been previously shown: that it is a substance receptive to form, and in itself it is neither continuous nor discontinuous, neither one nor many, nor disposed to occupation of space. Rather, these attributes attach to it from the form. And it is clear that since it does not in itself have position, those possibilities do not apply. The concomitance they prove is between form and matter, which they proved by demonstration, and it does not have position. So what need is there to refute that it has position? As for matter in the other sense, the discussion here is not about it, and it is far removed from the intention by stages, as is not hidden. Because it was said: The point has position and is not divisible. The answer is that what is meant is everything that has position independently, not dependently. The summary is that every substance having position is divisible, based on the previous refutation of the indivisible part.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof that matter (الهيولى) cannot exist without form (الصورة). The argument is a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil): if matter were stripped of form, it would either have position (وضع) or not. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, so the premise is false. The first possibility (having position) is examined: if it has position, it must be divisible or not. But everything with position is divisible, as established earlier in the refutation of the atom (الجزء الذي لا يتجزى). Thus matter would have to be divisible, but matter in its true nature is not divisible in itself. The second possibility (not having position) is also impossible because matter cannot be devoid of position if it is to be a substance? The text does not elaborate the second impossibility directly but concludes that both are impossible. The commentator then digresses to note that this entire discussion about position is unnecessary because the matter under consideration is not the kind that has position in itself; rather, it only acquires position from form. So the conventional proofs that matter is not positioned are sufficient, and there is no need to refute that it has position. An objection is raised: a point (نقطة) has position but is not divisible. The response is that 'having position' here means having position independently (بالاستقلال), not by dependence (بالتبع). A point has position only dependently as the end of a line, so it does not count. Therefore, every substance with independent position is divisible. The passage thus refines the understanding of matter and avoids unnecessary arguments.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الوضع
الجزء الذي لا يتجزى

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A conditional syllogism (modus ponens)
D) An inductive generalization

Key Takeaway
The core of the argument is a disjunctive syllogism where both branches of the disjunction are negated, forcing the conclusion that matter cannot exist without form.

The Objection of the Indivisible Point and Its Resolution
Commentator addresses the objection of the point having position without divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the objection about the point having position but not being divisible does not undermine the proof, and distinguish between independent and dependent position.

Core Idea
A point has position only dependently, so it does not refute the principle that every substance with independent position is divisible.

After the main proof, the commentator addresses a potential objection: a geometric point (nuqta) has position but is not divisible, which seems to contradict the claim that everything having position is divisible. The response draws a crucial distinction between having position independently (bi al-istiqlal) and dependently (bi al-taba'). This lesson examines this dialectical exchange and its implications for the logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the claim: the point has position but is not divisible.
2
Understand the challenge
This objection targets the premise used in the first branch: everything with position is divisible. If a point is a counterexample, the premise is false.
3
Examine the response
The commentator explains that 'having position' means having independent position; a point has position only as the endpoint of a line (dependently), so it is not a counterexample.
4
Test the distinction
Consider other cases: a line has independent position and is divisible. The point is not a substance; it is an accident. So the principle holds for substances.
5
Relate to the overall argument
This response preserves the validity of the main proof by clarifying the scope of the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof that matter (الهيولى) cannot exist without form (الصورة). The argument is a disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil): if matter were stripped of form, it would either have position (وضع) or not. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, so the premise is false. The first possibility (having position) is examined: if it has position, it must be divisible or not. But everything with position is divisible, as established earlier in the refutation of the atom (الجزء الذي لا يتجزى). Thus matter would have to be divisible, but matter in its true nature is not divisible in itself. The second possibility (not having position) is also impossible because matter cannot be devoid of position if it is to be a substance? The text does not elaborate the second impossibility directly but concludes that both are impossible. The commentator then digresses to note that this entire discussion about position is unnecessary because the matter under consideration is not the kind that has position in itself; rather, it only acquires position from form. So the conventional proofs that matter is not positioned are sufficient, and there is no need to refute that it has position. An objection is raised: a point (نقطة) has position but is not divisible. The response is that 'having position' here means having position independently (بالاستقلال), not by dependence (بالتبع). A point has position only dependently as the end of a line, so it does not count. Therefore, every substance with independent position is divisible. The passage thus refines the understanding of matter and avoids unnecessary arguments.

Key Terms
وضع بالاستقلال
وضع بالتبع
النقطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because a point is not a substance
B) Because a point has position only dependently, not independently
C) Because a point is divisible in reality
D) Because the principle only applies to bodies

Key Takeaway
The resolution hinges on the distinction between independent and dependent position, which saves the premise used in the main proof.

Substantial Form Is Not a Cause for Matter: A Refutation
Author argues that substantial form is not a cause for matter and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's conditional argument that substantial form is not a cause for prime matter and evaluate the objections and replies.

Core Idea
Substantial form and prime matter are interdependent but from separate causes; no circular causality.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument within Islamic Peripatetic philosophy (falsafa) that substantial form (al-sūrat al-naw'iyya) cannot be a cause for prime matter (al-hayūlā). The author systematically refutes efficient, receptive, and formal causality, then addresses two objections. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure of the argument, which uses conditional reasoning to expose contradictions and clarify the true relationship between form and matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis and types of causality
The author states that form is not a cause for matter. He then examines three types: efficient, receptive, and formal. Note that his primary target is efficient causality, as the others are dismissed more briefly.
Receptive causality is rejected because it would reverse the locus (form would receive matter). Formal causality fails because form is not a part of matter.
2
Understand the efficient causality argument
If form were an efficient cause of matter, form would be prior to matter. But form requires shape (al-shakl) for its individuation, and shape depends on matter as a receptive cause. Hence shape would also be prior to matter, contradicting the fact that shape does not exist before matter. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The argument is a conditional: if antecedent (form is efficient cause) then an impossibility follows, so antecedent is false.
3
Analyze the first objection and reply
Objection: Shape is posterior to form (by degrees), so it cannot be prior. Reply: The need is for the particular form (al-sūra al-shakhsiyya), not for the essence of form. The particular form requires shape for its individuation, and this does not imply priority of shape over the essence.
This distinction saves the argument from the objection about temporal priority.
4
Analyze the second objection and reply
Objection: Form's need for shape is false; if shape is particular, form persists despite shape changes (e.g., wax); if universal, it cannot individuate. Reply: 'Need' means form is never devoid of some individual shape, which serves as a sign (amāra) of individuation, not a part.
The author denies that shape is a constitutive part, rather it is an accompanying sign.
5
Conclude with interdependence without circularity
Matter needs form for actuality and persistence; form needs shape for individuation, and shape needs matter. Since the aspects of need are different (matter's need is for existence, form's need is for individuation), no circularity (dawr) arises.
The analogy of the ceiling and supports illustrates mutual dependence without causal priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والشكل لا يوجد قبل الهيولى؛ لأنها علة قابلية له، فلو كانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى لكانت متقدمة عليها، فيلزم تقدم الشكل عليها؛ لأن الشكل علة لتشخص الصورة أو مساوق له، فيلزم من تقدم الصورة تقدم علة الشكل متأخر عن الصورة بمراتب؛ لأنه متأخر عن الأطراف وهي متأخرة عن المقدار وهو عن الصورة، فكيف يتصور تقدمه عليها أو أن يكون معها؟ وأجيب عنه: أن المراد أن الصورة الشخصية محتاجة في تشخصها إلى الشكل، ولا بأس يحتاج الشيء في تشخصه إلى ما يحتاج إلى ماهيته والكلام الذي ذكره المعترض إنما يفيد تأخر الشكل عن ماهية الصورة ولا كلام فيه وإنما الكلام في الصورة الشخصية، وقد يقال: احتياج الصورة في تشخصها إلى الشكل باطل؛ لأنه إن كان المراد الشكل الجزئي فهو ليس بصحيح، كما ترى في الشمعة المتبدلة الأشكال بقائها بشخصها وفي الهواء المتكاثف والمتخلخل، وإن كان المراد الكلي، فلا يتصور أن يفيد تشخيص الصورة؛ لأن الكلي أمر مبهم فكيف يتعين به غيره؟ وأجيب عنه أن المراد شكل له والمراد بالاحتياج أن الصورة لا تخلو عن فرد ما، وهو من أمارات التشخص لا أنه جزء، ويجوز أن يكون الفرد المنتشر من الشكل أمارة له وليست واحدة منهما غنية عن الأخرى، أما الهيولى فلها احتياج إليها في تحصلها ووجودها بالشكل، والشكل لا يوجد قبل الهيولى، فلو كانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى لكانت متقدمة على الهيولى هذا خلف، فإذن وجود كل منهما عن سبب منفصل، وليست الهيولى غنية عن الصورة من كل الوجوه؛ لما بينا أنها لا تقوم بالفعل بدون الصورة، وليست الصورة أيضا غنية عن الهيولى من كل الوجوه؛ لما بينا أنها لا توجد بدون الشكل المفتقر إلى الهيولى، فالهيولي تفتقر إلى الصورة في بقائها، والصورة مفتقرة إلى الهيولى في تشكلها. وهو الصورة، فمثلها مثل السقف يحتاج في وجوده وبقائه إلى طبيعة الدعامات يستحفظ بتعاقب أفرادها عليه والصورة لها احتياج إلى الهيولى في تشكلها، وحاصل الكلام أن الهيولى محتاجة في وجودها وبقائها، والصورة في تشخصها، ولما كان جهة الاحتياج مختلفتين ما لزم الدور.
Section on substantial form. Also it is not a cause for matter, because form only exists with shape or it is not a cause etc. The summary is that the particular form exists by shape if shape is a cause for its individuation, or with shape if shape is concomitant with its individuation, and shape does not exist before matter because matter is a receptive cause for it. So if form were a cause for the existence of matter, it would be prior to it, necessitating the priority of shape over it because shape is a cause for the individuation of form or concomitant with it. So the priority of form would necessitate the priority of its cause of individuation or what is concomitant with it, but shape does not exist before matter. This is a contradiction. And by cause is meant efficient cause, because denying receptive cause is obvious and needless of explanation; because the receptive cause is a locus for the recipient, so if it were a receptive cause for matter, then form would be a locus for matter, which has been shown to be false. Rather, it has been proven by demonstration that matter is the locus for form. Nor is it a formal cause, because that would be a part of the thing, and form is not a part of matter but external to it and attached to it, as established. And it might be said: shape is posterior to form by degrees because it is posterior to the extremities, which are posterior to quantity, which is posterior to form; so how can it be imagined to be prior to it or simultaneous with it? The answer: what is meant is that the particular form needs shape for its individuation, and there is no harm in something needing for its individuation something that needs its essence. The objector's speech only implies the posteriority of shape to the essence of form, and there is no dispute about that; the dispute is about the particular form. And it might be said: the need of form for shape in its individuation is false; because if what is meant is the particular shape, it is not valid, as you see in the wax that changes shapes while remaining its person, and in rarefied and condensed air. If what is meant is the universal, then it cannot be conceived to individuate the form, because the universal is an ambiguous thing, so how can it determine something else? The answer: what is meant is a shape for it, and by need is meant that form is never devoid of some individual shape, which is one of the signs of individuation, not a part. And it is possible that the distributed individual of shape is a sign for it. Neither is independent of the other. As for matter, it has a need for it in its attainment and existence through shape, and shape does not exist before matter. So if form were a cause for the existence of matter, it would be prior to matter; this is a contradiction. Therefore, the existence of each is from a separate cause. Matter is not independent of form in every aspect, because we have shown that it does not subsist in actuality without form. Nor is form independent of matter in every aspect, because we have shown that it does not exist without shape, which is in need of matter. So matter needs form for its persistence, and form needs matter for its configuration. And that is form; its example is like the ceiling, which in its existence and persistence needs the nature of supports, preserving itself by the succession of its individuals upon it. And form has need of matter for its configuration. The upshot is that matter is needy in its existence and persistence, and form is needy in its individuation. And since the aspects of need are different, no circularity occurs.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the idea that substantial form (الصورة النوعية) is a cause for prime matter (الهيولى). The author begins by stating the thesis: form is not a cause for matter. The reason given is that form's existence is tied to shape (الشكل), which itself depends on matter. The argument proceeds by considering three types of causality: efficient, receptive, and formal. For efficient causality, the author argues that if form were an efficient cause of matter, it would have to be prior to matter, but shape (which is necessary for form) is posterior to matter, leading to a contradiction. For receptive causality, the author notes that it is obvious form cannot be a receptive cause because that would require form to be a locus for matter, whereas the opposite is demonstrated. For formal causality, form cannot be part of matter because it is external. The passage then addresses two objections. The first objection claims shape is posterior to form, so it cannot be prior; the author responds that the need is for the particular form, not the essence. The second objection says form's need for shape is false because either shape is particular (but form persists despite shape changes) or universal (which cannot individuate); the author responds that need means form never lacks some individual shape, which is a sign of individuation, not a part. The conclusion is that matter and form are interdependent but from separate causes, and their needs are different aspects, so no circularity (دور) arises.

الهيولى
الشكل

Defining Time as Quantity of Motion
Definition of time as quantity of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the definition of time as quantity of motion using genus and differentia and verify its completeness.

Core Idea
Time is the measure of motion, not a stationary quantity.

This lesson unpacks the definition of time provided by the author. The author argues that time is a quantity (miqdar) but not for a stationary state (hay'ah qarrah); rather, it is for a non-stationary state, which is motion. Understanding this definition requires distinguishing between stationary and non-stationary quantities and recognizing that time's proper genus is 'quantity' and its differentia is 'of motion'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. The text begins by discussing what time (الزمان) is not, then defines it positively.
The definiendum is 'al-zaman' (time).
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does the author place time in? The text says 'لا يكون مقدارا' and then 'فهو مقدار', so the genus is 'quantity' (مقدار).
Quantity here means measure or amount.
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes time from other quantities? The differentia is that it is the quantity of a non-stationary state (هيئة غير قارة), which is then identified as motion (الحركة).
The differentia is 'of motion' (للحركة).
4
Assess definition type
Is this a complete definition (hadd haqiqi) or a description (rasm)? The author uses a syllogism to derive the definition, indicating that genus and differentia are explicitly given, making it a hadd haqiqi.
The author states 'وهو المطلوب' (that is what is sought), affirming the definition's adequacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول فيما يعم الأجسام
فصل في الزمان
قارا لا يكون مقدارا لهيئة قارة، فهو مقدار لهيئة غير قارة، وكل هيئة غير قارة فهي الحركة، فالزمان مقدار الحركة، وهو المطلوب، ونقول أيضا: إن الزمان لا بداية له ولا نهاية له؛ لأنه لو كان له بداية لكان عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية، وكل قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية فهي زمانية، فيكون قبل الزمان زمان هذا خلف ولو كان له نهاية لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا توجد مع القبلية، فتكون زمانية، فيكون بعد الزمان زمان هذا خلف.
-
فظاهرة، وأما الكبرى؛ فلأنه لو كان الأمر غير القار مقدارا لشيء قار للزم وجود ذلك الشيء بدونه، والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله، أما بطلان التالي فلأن مقدار الشيء من لوازم الشيء، كما ترى أن مقدار الأجسام من لوازمها لا يخلو جسم ما عن مقداره. :مقدارا: لأن مقدار الشيء يكون لازما له كمقدار الجسم له، ولو كان المقدار الغير القار قائما بشيء قار يلزم وجود ذلك الشيء بدونه. لا بداية له: قد يظن من أزلية الزمان وأبديته أن الزمان واجب الوجود، وهذا الظن فاسد؛ لأن الواجب ما يمتنع عليه جميع أنحاء العدم بالنظر إلى ذاته لا ما يمتنع عليه النحو الخاص من العدم والزمان إنما يمتنع عليه العدم السابق على الوجود اللاحق، ولا يلزم من امتناع العدم المقيد بالقبلية والبعدية امتناع مطلق العدم حتى يلزم كونه واجب الوجود، والزمان من أضعف الموجودات حيث لا قرار له، فكيف يكون واجب الوجود الذي هو أقوى الموجودات بقاء وثباتا؟
لو كان إلخ : هذا قياس الخلف أي لو فرض عدم الزمان قبل وجوده للزم وجوده حين العدم، وبيان الملازمة: أنه لو كان للزمان بداية لكان عدمه قبل وجوده، وهذه القبلية لا توجد مع البعدية، وإلا لزم اجتماع النقيضين وكل قبلية كذلك فهي زمانية، فقبلية العدم أيضا زمانية فيلزم وجود الزمان حين ما فرض عدمه، وهذا خلف،
وإنما لزم هذا الخلف لفرض بداية الزمان، فتكون بداية الزمان محالا، فثبت ما ادعيناه من أن الزمان ليس له بداية، وكذلك البيان في أن الزمان ليس له نهاية.
The first art concerning that which is common to bodies. Chapter on time. 'Qara' is not a quantity for a stationary state, so it is a quantity for a non-stationary state, and every non-stationary state is motion, so time is the quantity of motion, and that is what is sought. And we also say: time has no beginning and no end; because if it had a beginning, its non-existence before its existence would be a priority that does not exist with posteriority, and every priority that does not exist with posteriority is temporal, so there would be a time before time – this is a contradiction. And if it had an end, its non-existence after its existence would be a posteriority that does not exist with priority, so it would be temporal, so there would be a time after time – this is a contradiction. – Al-Zahira? And as for the major premise: because if the non-stationary thing were a quantity for a stationary thing, it would necessitate the existence of that thing without it, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the falsity of the consequent: because the quantity of a thing is among its necessary concomitants, as you see that the quantity of bodies is among their necessary concomitants; no body is devoid of its quantity. ':quantity:' Because the quantity of a thing is necessary for it, like the quantity of a body for it; and if the non-stationary quantity were subsistent in a stationary thing, it would necessitate the existence of that thing without it. 'Has no beginning:' One might think from the eternality a parte ante and a parte post of time that time is necessary of existence, but this thought is corrupt; because the necessary is that for which all aspects of non-existence are impossible with respect to its essence, not that for which a specific aspect of non-existence is impossible; time only has impossibility of non-existence prior to subsequent existence, and from the impossibility of non-existence restricted to priority and posteriority it does not follow the impossibility of absolute non-existence so that it would be necessary of existence. And time is among the weakest of existents, having no stability, so how could it be the necessary of existence, which is the strongest of existents in permanence and constancy? 'If it were, etc.': This is a reductio argument: if we suppose the non-existence of time before its existence, its existence would be necessary at the time of non-existence. The explanation of the entailment: if time had a beginning, its non-existence would be before its existence, and this priority does not exist with posteriority, otherwise the union of two contradictories would be necessary; and every such priority is temporal, so the priority of non-existence is also temporal, so the existence of time would be necessary at the time when it was supposed non-existent, and this is a contradiction. And this contradiction only follows from the assumption of time having a beginning, so the beginning of time is impossible; thus we have proven what we claimed: that time has no beginning, and similarly the proof that time has no end.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines time and proves its eternity a parte ante and a parte post. The author first states that time is a quantity for a non-stationary state (hay'ah ghayr qarrah), meaning it is the measure of motion, since motion is the only non-stationary state. This definition is presented as a syllogism: time is a quantity for a non-stationary state, and every non-stationary state is motion, therefore time is the quantity of motion. Then the author turns to proving that time has no beginning and no end using a reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf). The proof: if time had a beginning, then before its existence there would be a priority (qabliyyah) that does not coexist with posteriority; such priority is temporal, implying a time before time, which is contradictory. The same reasoning applies to the end. The commentary clarifies the major premise, explaining that a non-stationary quantity cannot subsist in a stationary thing because quantity is a necessary concomitant; thus the quantity must belong to something non-stationary. It also refutes the misconception that time's eternity implies it is necessary existent (wajib al-wujud). The commentator argues that necessary existence means impossibility of all aspects of non-existence, not just one specific aspect like prior non-existence. Time only lacks prior non-existence, but could still be contingent. Furthermore, time is weak, whereas the Necessary Existent is the strongest. The proof is then restated in a formal reductio: assuming a beginning leads to a contradiction, so the beginning is impossible, and similarly for the end.

الزمان
الحركة
مقدار

B) Quantity

Time is defined as the quantity of motion, giving genus (quantity) and differentia (of motion), making it a complete essential definition.
