Proving Time Has No Beginning or End
Proof of time's eternity via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum proof that time has no beginning and no end, and distinguish it from claims of necessary existence.

Core Idea
Time's eternity is proven by showing a beginning or end leads to contradiction.

This lesson examines the author's proof for the eternity of time using reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf). The author argues that if time had a beginning, then there would be a time before time, which is contradictory. The same logic applies to an end. The commentary also clarifies that this does not make time necessary existent, as only a specific non-existence is impossible.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption to be refuted
The author assumes the opposite of what is to be proven: that time has a beginning (له بداية). Identify this assumption in the Arabic.
Similarly for the end, the assumption is that time has an end (له نهاية).
2
Derive the consequence
From the assumption, the author derives that there would be a priority (قبلية) of non-existence over existence that does not coexist with posteriority. This priority is claimed to be temporal, leading to a time before time.
The key is that such a priority is inherently temporal.
3
Identify the contradiction
The consequence 'time before time' is impossible (خلف). This contradicts the assumption that time has a beginning.
The same reasoning is applied symmetrically to the end.
4
Conclude the negation of the assumption
Since the assumption leads to a contradiction, it must be false. Therefore, time has no beginning and no end.
This is a valid reductio ad absurdum.
5
Address the misconception
The commentator clarifies that time's lack of beginning does not make it necessary existent, because necessary existence requires impossibility of all non-existence, not just prior non-existence.
Time is actually a weak existent, not strong like the Necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول فيما يعم الأجسام
فصل في الزمان
قارا لا يكون مقدارا لهيئة قارة، فهو مقدار لهيئة غير قارة، وكل هيئة غير قارة فهي الحركة، فالزمان مقدار الحركة، وهو المطلوب، ونقول أيضا: إن الزمان لا بداية له ولا نهاية له؛ لأنه لو كان له بداية لكان عدمه قبل وجوده قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية، وكل قبلية لا توجد مع البعدية فهي زمانية، فيكون قبل الزمان زمان هذا خلف ولو كان له نهاية لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا توجد مع القبلية، فتكون زمانية، فيكون بعد الزمان زمان هذا خلف.
-
فظاهرة، وأما الكبرى؛ فلأنه لو كان الأمر غير القار مقدارا لشيء قار للزم وجود ذلك الشيء بدونه، والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله، أما بطلان التالي فلأن مقدار الشيء من لوازم الشيء، كما ترى أن مقدار الأجسام من لوازمها لا يخلو جسم ما عن مقداره. :مقدارا: لأن مقدار الشيء يكون لازما له كمقدار الجسم له، ولو كان المقدار الغير القار قائما بشيء قار يلزم وجود ذلك الشيء بدونه. لا بداية له: قد يظن من أزلية الزمان وأبديته أن الزمان واجب الوجود، وهذا الظن فاسد؛ لأن الواجب ما يمتنع عليه جميع أنحاء العدم بالنظر إلى ذاته لا ما يمتنع عليه النحو الخاص من العدم والزمان إنما يمتنع عليه العدم السابق على الوجود اللاحق، ولا يلزم من امتناع العدم المقيد بالقبلية والبعدية امتناع مطلق العدم حتى يلزم كونه واجب الوجود، والزمان من أضعف الموجودات حيث لا قرار له، فكيف يكون واجب الوجود الذي هو أقوى الموجودات بقاء وثباتا؟
لو كان إلخ : هذا قياس الخلف أي لو فرض عدم الزمان قبل وجوده للزم وجوده حين العدم، وبيان الملازمة: أنه لو كان للزمان بداية لكان عدمه قبل وجوده، وهذه القبلية لا توجد مع البعدية، وإلا لزم اجتماع النقيضين وكل قبلية كذلك فهي زمانية، فقبلية العدم أيضا زمانية فيلزم وجود الزمان حين ما فرض عدمه، وهذا خلف،
وإنما لزم هذا الخلف لفرض بداية الزمان، فتكون بداية الزمان محالا، فثبت ما ادعيناه من أن الزمان ليس له بداية، وكذلك البيان في أن الزمان ليس له نهاية.
The first art concerning that which is common to bodies. Chapter on time. 'Qara' is not a quantity for a stationary state, so it is a quantity for a non-stationary state, and every non-stationary state is motion, so time is the quantity of motion, and that is what is sought. And we also say: time has no beginning and no end; because if it had a beginning, its non-existence before its existence would be a priority that does not exist with posteriority, and every priority that does not exist with posteriority is temporal, so there would be a time before time – this is a contradiction. And if it had an end, its non-existence after its existence would be a posteriority that does not exist with priority, so it would be temporal, so there would be a time after time – this is a contradiction. – Al-Zahira? And as for the major premise: because if the non-stationary thing were a quantity for a stationary thing, it would necessitate the existence of that thing without it, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the falsity of the consequent: because the quantity of a thing is among its necessary concomitants, as you see that the quantity of bodies is among their necessary concomitants; no body is devoid of its quantity. ':quantity:' Because the quantity of a thing is necessary for it, like the quantity of a body for it; and if the non-stationary quantity were subsistent in a stationary thing, it would necessitate the existence of that thing without it. 'Has no beginning:' One might think from the eternality a parte ante and a parte post of time that time is necessary of existence, but this thought is corrupt; because the necessary is that for which all aspects of non-existence are impossible with respect to its essence, not that for which a specific aspect of non-existence is impossible; time only has impossibility of non-existence prior to subsequent existence, and from the impossibility of non-existence restricted to priority and posteriority it does not follow the impossibility of absolute non-existence so that it would be necessary of existence. And time is among the weakest of existents, having no stability, so how could it be the necessary of existence, which is the strongest of existents in permanence and constancy? 'If it were, etc.': This is a reductio argument: if we suppose the non-existence of time before its existence, its existence would be necessary at the time of non-existence. The explanation of the entailment: if time had a beginning, its non-existence would be before its existence, and this priority does not exist with posteriority, otherwise the union of two contradictories would be necessary; and every such priority is temporal, so the priority of non-existence is also temporal, so the existence of time would be necessary at the time when it was supposed non-existent, and this is a contradiction. And this contradiction only follows from the assumption of time having a beginning, so the beginning of time is impossible; thus we have proven what we claimed: that time has no beginning, and similarly the proof that time has no end.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines time and proves its eternity a parte ante and a parte post. The author first states that time is a quantity for a non-stationary state (hay'ah ghayr qarrah), meaning it is the measure of motion, since motion is the only non-stationary state. This definition is presented as a syllogism: time is a quantity for a non-stationary state, and every non-stationary state is motion, therefore time is the quantity of motion. Then the author turns to proving that time has no beginning and no end using a reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf). The proof: if time had a beginning, then before its existence there would be a priority (qabliyyah) that does not coexist with posteriority; such priority is temporal, implying a time before time, which is contradictory. The same reasoning applies to the end. The commentary clarifies the major premise, explaining that a non-stationary quantity cannot subsist in a stationary thing because quantity is a necessary concomitant; thus the quantity must belong to something non-stationary. It also refutes the misconception that time's eternity implies it is necessary existent (wajib al-wujud). The commentator argues that necessary existence means impossibility of all aspects of non-existence, not just one specific aspect like prior non-existence. Time only lacks prior non-existence, but could still be contingent. Furthermore, time is weak, whereas the Necessary Existent is the strongest. The proof is then restated in a formal reductio: assuming a beginning leads to a contradiction, so the beginning is impossible, and similarly for the end.

Key Terms
الزمان
قياس الخلف
قبلية
بعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the impossibility of time having a beginning not entail that time is necessary existent?
A) Because time is a weakest existent, not a strongest.
B) Because the necessary existent must be impossible in all aspects of non-existence, while time only lacks prior non-existence.
C) Because time is a quantity, and quantities cannot be necessary.
D) Because the proof is only about beginning, not about existence.

Key Takeaway
Time's eternity is proven via reductio: assuming a beginning or end leads to a contradiction, so time must be eternal, but this does not make it necessary existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Rephrase the proof that time has no beginning as a formal reductio ad absurdum: state the assumption, the derived contradiction, and the conclusion.
?
Apply the same reductio structure to prove that the world cannot have a beginning if it is created ex nihilo (if possible, based on kalam arguments).

Defining the Sphere and the Six Directions
Author defines sphere and explains the six directions, distinguishing absolute and relative directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six directions of a body, classify them as absolute or relative, and explain why the celestial sphere is considered spherical based on its definition.

Core Idea
Above and below are absolute; front, back, right, left are relative to the observer.

This passage lays the geometric foundation for Islamic cosmology. The celestial sphere is defined as a perfect sphere, and directions are analyzed in terms of a body's three dimensions. Understanding which directions are absolute and which are relative is crucial for later discussions on the structure of the universe and for practical applications like determining the qibla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of a spherical body
The passage defines a spherical body as one with a center point such that all lines from the center to the surface are equal. Note that this definition is applied to the celestial sphere (al-falak).
The term 'mustadir' is used for spherical.
2
Extract the three dimensions and their directional endpoints
The author states that a body has three dimensions (length, width, depth), each with two endpoints, giving six directions. Identify the pairs: above/below (longitudinal), right/left (latitudinal), front/back (depth).
These are based on a human standing upright.
3
Distinguish absolute from relative directions
The passage explicitly says that above and below are unchanging and do not switch when a person is inverted, while the other four directions are relative and change with orientation.
Above is towards the head, below towards the feet, regardless of orientation.
4
Apply the definition of direction (jihah) to bodies
Direction is defined as the endpoint of an imaginary extension from a pointer. For bodies, it means the ends of the dimensions. Connect this to the spherical definition: the sphere's center is the reference point for directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في الفلكيات
فصل في إثبات كون الفلك مستديرا
بيانه أن ههنا جهتين لا تتبدلان إحداهما فوق والأخرى تحت، وكل
مستديرا: الجسم المستدير هو الجسم الذي يفرض في وسطه نقطة، ويكون جميع الخطوط الخارجة منها متساوية ويقال لتلك النقطة مركز، ولسطح ذلك الجسم محيط، وللجسم كرة، هكذا الفلك.
جهتين: الجهة عبارة عن طرف الامتداد الموهوم الآخذ من المشير المنتهى إلى المشار إليه، وهي قد تضاف إلى الإشارة، فيقال: جهة الإشارة، ويراد بها منتهى الإشارة، وقد تضاف إلى الحركة فيقال: جهة الحركة ويراد بها ما منه الحركة أو ما إليه الحركة، وقد تضاف إلى الأجسام وسائر الأبعاد من الخط والسطح، فيراد بها نهاية الجسم أو البعد والجسم؛ إذ هو ذو أبعاد ثلاثة، ولكل بعد طرفان فله جهات ست، فطرفا الامتداد الطولي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار طول قامته حين هو قائم فوقا وتحتا، فالفوق ما يلي رأسه بالطبع، والتحت ما يلي قدمه بالطبع، وطرفا الامتداد العرضي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار عرض قامته باليمين والشمال وطرفا الامتداد العمقي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار عرض قامته بالقدام والخلف وقد تطلق الجهة على ما يلي النهاية، فالذي يلي وجهه قداما، وما يلي ظهره يسمى خلفا وما يلي يمينه يمينا، وما يلي شماله شمالا، وهذه الجهات الأربع إضافية تسمى بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، ولذا إذا تحول من وجهه إلى ظهره صار ما كان من قبل خلفا ،قداما، وما كان قداما صار خلفا، وهكذا في اليمين واليسار، وأما جهتا الفوق والتحت فلا تتبدلان إذا تنكس الإنسان بل يقال للقدم -
The second art concerning celestial matters. Chapter on proving that the sphere is spherical. Its explanation: that here are two directions that do not change: one is above and the other is below, and every... 'Spherical': The spherical body is the body in whose middle a point is supposed, and all lines emerging from it are equal; that point is called center, the surface of that body is called circumference, and the body is called a sphere; thus is the celestial sphere. 'Two directions': Direction is the term of the imaginary extension taken from the pointer to the point indicated. It may be attributed to indication, so it is said 'direction of indication' meaning the endpoint of indication; it may be attributed to motion, so it is said 'direction of motion' meaning that from which motion originates or that to which motion goes; and it may be attributed to bodies and other dimensions like line and surface, meaning the end of the body or dimension. Since the body has three dimensions, and each dimension has two ends, it has six directions: the ends of the longitudinal extension are called by man, considering the length of his stature when he is upright, 'above' and 'below': the above is what is naturally towards his head, the below what is naturally towards his feet. The ends of the latitudinal extension he calls, considering the width of his stature, 'right' and 'left'. The ends of the depth extension he calls, considering the width of his stature, 'front' and 'back'. And 'direction' may be applied to what is near the end; so what is near his face is called front, what is near his back is called back, what is near his right is called right, what is near his left is called left. These four directions are relative, named in relation to man; hence if he turns from his face to his back, what was front becomes back, what was back becomes front, and likewise for right and left. As for the two directions of above and below, they do not change if man is inverted; rather the foot is said to be...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the celestial sphere (al-falak) and explains the concept of direction (jihah). The author begins by stating that there are two unchanging directions: above and below. Then the commentary defines a spherical body: a sphere is a body with a center point such that all lines from the center to the surface are equal; the center is called 'markaz', the surface 'muhit', and the body 'kurah'. The celestial sphere is spherical. Next, the term 'direction' is analyzed. Direction is defined as the endpoint of an imaginary extension from a pointer to the thing pointed at. Direction can be attributed to indication, motion, or bodies. For bodies, since they have three dimensions (length, width, depth), each with two ends, there are six directions: above/below (from longitudinal extension), right/left (from latitudinal), and front/back (from depth). These are named relative to a human standing upright: above is towards the head, below towards feet; right/left relative to the body's width; front/back relative to the front and back. The four directions (right, left, front, back) are relative: they change when the person turns. However, above and below are absolute and do not change even if a person is inverted – the foot remains below and the head above. The text ends abruptly but clearly establishes the distinction.

Key Terms
الفلك (al-falak): The celestial sphere, defined as a spherical body with a center and equal radii. [Note: Stable term in astronomy.]
الجهة (al-jihah): Direction, defined as the endpoint of an imaginary extension from a pointer. It has various applications: indication, motion, or bodies. [Note: No significant shift; standard in physics.]
المستدير (al-mustadir): Spherical; a body with a center and equal radii. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Front and back
B) Right and left
C) Above and below
D) All six directions are relative

Key Takeaway
In Islamic physics, directions are rooted in the geometry of bodies: above and below are absolute, while front, back, right, left are relative to the observer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Analyzing a Disjunctive Dilemma: The Celestial Sphere
Proof that the celestial sphere is a simple sphere via dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a disjunctive dilemma in an Islamic philosophical argument and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
A dilemma eliminates all possibilities to establish a single necessary conclusion.

How do you prove that something must be simple? One powerful method is the dilemma: if all options lead to contradictions, the only remaining explanation must be true. The philosopher here uses a disjunctive syllogism to argue that the celestial sphere cannot be composed of independent spheres or non-spheres, so it must be a single simple spherical body. Let's break down this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dilemma's two options
The author presents two mutually exclusive possibilities: either each part of the celestial sphere is a sphere, or each part is not a sphere. Write down these two disjuncts.
Disjunct 1: each part is a sphere. Disjunct 2: each part is not a sphere.
2
Analyze the first option
If each part is a sphere, the author argues that their sum cannot form a continuous spherical surface. Why? Because spheres cannot fill space without gaps; they only touch at points. So this option leads to an impossibility (the celestial sphere would not be a continuous sphere).
This is a metaphysical impossibility given the nature of spheres.
3
Analyze the second option
If each part is not a sphere, then each part would naturally seek its own natural shape through rectilinear motion. This would make the whole celestial sphere subject to rectilinear motion. But the celestial sphere determines directions and cannot undergo rectilinear motion; that would be a contradiction.
The celestial sphere is the reference for all motion; it cannot itself move rectilinearly.
4
Draw the conclusion using disjunctive syllogism
Both disjuncts lead to contradictions, so the only remaining possibility is that the celestial sphere is a single simple body whose parts are not independent. In logical terms: either P or Q; P is false; Q is false; therefore, neither P nor Q, but the original assumption that the sphere is composite is false, so it must be simple. This is a reductio ad absurdum disguised as a dilemma.
The conclusion is implicit: the sphere is a simple body (basīṭ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك قابل للبسيط هو الكرة، ولو كان كل واحد منها كرة لاستحال أن يحصل من مجموعها سطح كري متصل الأجزاء، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأنه لو لم يكن كل واحد منها كرة، فيكون طالبا للشكل الطبعي، فيكون قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، هذا خلف.
Chapter that the celestial sphere is receptive to the simple body, which is the sphere. And if each one of them were a sphere, it would be impossible for a continuous spherical surface to result from their sum. And it is not possible to take the second [option]; because if each one of them were not a sphere, then it would be seeking the natural shape, and would be receptive to rectilinear motion; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma argument about the nature of the celestial sphere's parts. The author states that the sphere is a simple body. He then argues: if each part were a separate sphere, they could not form a continuous spherical surface upon aggregation, which is what the sphere requires. If, on the other hand, the parts are not spheres, then each part would naturally seek its own natural shape through rectilinear motion, which would make the whole sphere subject to rectilinear motion. But the celestial sphere determines directions and cannot undergo rectilinear motion, so that is impossible. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is that the sphere is a single simple spherical body whose parts are not independent spheres. This argument uses a classical logical dilemma (qiyas munfasil) to eliminate both sides and establish the simple nature of the celestial sphere.

Key Terms
فلك
كرة
بسيط
خلف

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive dilemma can effectively eliminate all possibilities to establish a definition, as shown in proving the celestial sphere is a simple body.

Reconstructing the Philosopher's Syllogism for the Sphere's Incorruptibility
Philosopher's syllogism proving sphere's incorruptibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving that the sphere does not undergo generation or corruption and identify its major and minor premises.

Core Idea
The sphere’s immutability is deduced from its role as a boundary of directions and the necessity of rectilinear motion for change.

The philosophers argue that the celestial sphere, unlike sublunary bodies, does not undergo generation (kawm) or corruption (fasad). Their proof is a categorical syllogism: the sphere is a definer of directions, and no definer of directions accepts generation or corruption. We will break down this argument step by step, examining how the major premise is justified and why the conclusion follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
The author states: 'The sphere does not accept generation and corruption.' This is the claim to be proved.
2
Find the middle term (hadd al-awsat)
The middle term is 'definer of directions' (muhaddid al-jihat). It appears in both the minor and major premises.
The minor premise is 'the sphere is a definer of directions' (given earlier).
3
Extract the major premise
The major premise is: 'Nothing that defines directions accepts generation or corruption.' This is supported by the sub-argument: anything that accepts generation/corruption has two natural places and thus requires rectilinear motion, but the sphere only has circular motion.
This major premise is universal negative (E).
4
Reconstruct the syllogism and check validity
Syllogism: Major premise: No definer of directions accepts generation/corruption. Minor premise: The sphere is a definer of directions. Conclusion: The sphere does not accept generation/corruption. This is a first-figure (Celarent) syllogism, which is valid.
The argument is valid on its own premises; the commentator does not dispute the logic but rejects the premises based on revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الفلك لا يقبل الكون والفساد والخرق والالتيام أما أنه لا يقبل الكون والفساد فلأنه محدد الجهات، ولا شيء من محدد الجهات يقبل الكون والفساد، أما الصغرى، فقد مر تقريرها، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل ما يقبل الكون والفساد فلصورته الحادثة حيز طبعي ولصورته الفاسدة حيز آخر طبعي؛ لما بينا أن كل جسم فله حيز طبعي، وكل ما هذا شأنه فهو قابل للحركة المستقيمة؛ لأن الصورة لا يقبل إلخ اعلم أن من الأجسام ما يقبل الكون والفساد كالعناصر، وسيجيء في العنصريات أن العناصر بعضها ينقلب بعضا بأن يفسد صورته ويحدث صورة أخرى، ومنها ما لا يقبل الكون والفساد والأفلاك عند الفلاسفة مما لا يقبل الكون والفساد وعندنا أهل الإسلام كل جسم سواء كان فلكا أو عنصرا قابل للكون والفساد والخرق والالتيام وقد نطق الكتاب بخرق السماء يوم القيامة، وأما اليوم، فما لها من فطور كما دل عليه الكتاب أيضا. فله حيز لأن الحيز الطبعي ما يقتضيه صورته النوعية ولما كانت الصورتان مختلفتين، كان حيزاهما مختلفين أيضا؛ لامتناع اجتماع جسمين في حيز واحد، وإلا لزم التداخل. هذا شأنه كل جسم يتوارد عليه الصورتان فذلك الجسم قابل للحركة المستقيمة؛ لأن الصورة الكائنة لا تخلو إما أن تكون في حيز طبعي أو في حيز غريب أي غير طبعي، فإن كانت في حيز غريب كانت تقتضي أن تذهب بالحركة المستقيمة إلى حيزها الطبعي؛ لأن الحركة المستديرة لا تقتضي بقاء الجسم في ذلك الحيز، فيلزم كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة وهو باطل، وإن كانت في حيز طبعي فالصورة الفاسدة كانت حين وجودها في حيز غريب فكانت تقتضي حين وجودها أن تخرج ذلك الحيز الغريب وتروح إلى الحيز الطبعي وما ذلك إلا بالحركة المستقيمة، فيلزم أيضا كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، وهو كما ترى.
Section on the sphere not accepting generation and corruption and tearing and repair. As for not accepting generation and corruption, because it is a definer of directions, and nothing that defines directions accepts generation and corruption. As for the minor premise, its verification has passed; as for the major premise, because everything that accepts generation and corruption has for its newly occurring form a natural place and for its corrupted form another natural place, since we have shown that every body has a natural place, and everything with this property is subject to rectilinear motion, because the form... does not accept, etc. Know that among bodies are those that accept generation and corruption like the elements, and it will come in the elementics that some elements change into others by corrupting their form and generating another form. And among them are those that do not accept generation and corruption, and the spheres according to the philosophers are among those that do not accept generation and corruption. But according to us, the people of Islam, every body, whether sphere or element, is subject to generation and corruption and tearing and repair. Indeed, the Book has spoken of the tearing of the sky on the Day of Resurrection, and today there is no crack in it, as the Book also indicates. So it has a place because the natural place is what its specific form necessitates, and since the two forms are different, their places are also different, because it is impossible for two bodies to be in one place, otherwise interpenetration would occur. Such is every body that is subject to the succession of two forms: that body is subject to rectilinear motion, because the coming-to-be form is either in its natural place or in an alien place, i.e., non-natural. If it is in an alien place, it necessitates that it go by rectilinear motion to its natural place, because circular motion does not necessitate keeping the body in that place, so it would follow that the sphere is subject to rectilinear motion, which is false. And if it is in its natural place, the decaying form was at the time of its existence in an alien place, so it demanded at the time of its existence to leave that alien place and go to the natural place, and that is only by rectilinear motion. So it would also follow that the sphere is subject to rectilinear motion, and that is as you see.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' demonstration that the celestial sphere does not undergo generation (kawm) and corruption (fasad), followed by a commentator's critical refutation from an Islamic perspective. The proof runs as follows: the sphere is a boundary of directions (muhaddid al-jihat). No boundary of directions accepts generation or corruption. The minor premise (that the sphere is a boundary of directions) has been established earlier. The major premise is argued: anything that undergoes generation and corruption has a natural place (hayyiz tabi'i) for its new form and a different natural place for its old form. Since every body has a natural place, such a body would require rectilinear motion (to move from one natural place to another), but the sphere only has circular motion. Therefore, the sphere cannot undergo generation or corruption. The commentator then intervenes: 'Know that among bodies are those that accept generation and corruption (like the elements) and those that do not—the spheres according to the philosophers. But according to us Muslims, every body, whether sphere or element, is subject to generation, corruption, tearing (kharq), and repair (iltiyam). The Qur'an mentions the tearing of the sky on the Day of Judgment, and that today there is no crack.' The commentator then elaborates: the sphere would have a natural place for a new form, which would require rectilinear motion, contradicting the sphere's circular motion. Thus, the philosopher's argument is sound on their premises, but the Islamic view, based on revelation, overrules it. The lesson ends with a summary: if the new form were in its natural place, the old form must have been in an alien place, necessitating rectilinear motion to leave it. Hence, the sphere cannot accept generation/corruption on the philosophers' assumptions.

Key Terms
كون وفساد (kawm wa fasad): Generation (coming into being) and corruption (passing away), applicable to sublunary bodies according to philosophers. [Note: In Islamic theology, all bodies including spheres are created and will perish.]
خرق والتيام (kharq wa iltiyam): Tearing (cracking) and repair (mending), which the sphere does not undergo according to philosophers but does according to Islam (e.g., at the Day of Judgment).
حيز طبعي (hayyiz tabi'i): Natural place of a body, determined by its specific form.
محدد الجهات (muhaddid al-jihat): A definer of directions; the sphere is said to bound the six directions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the major premise in the philosopher's argument against the sphere undergoing generation and corruption?
A) The sphere is eternal and unchangeable.
B) Nothing that defines directions accepts generation or corruption.
C) The sphere is made of a fifth element.
D) Everything that accepts generation/corruption requires rectilinear motion.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof that the sphere is incorruptible is a formally valid categorical syllogism relying on the unique status of the sphere as a boundary of directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critiquing the Philosophers’ Doctrine: The Islamic View on the Sphere’s Mutability
Commentator's Islamic refutation of philosophers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the commentator's critique of the philosopher's doctrine and identify the basis (revelation) for the Islamic alternative.

Core Idea
Revelation overrides natural reasoning: the Qur’ān’s description of the sky’s tearing contradicts the philosopher’s claim of celestial incorruptibility.

After presenting the philosophers' syllogism, the commentator introduces the Islamic position: every body, including the sphere, is subject to generation, corruption, tearing, and repair. The basis is not logical deduction but explicit scriptural evidence. We will analyze how the commentator uses revelation to refute the philosophical claim and what this tells us about the relationship between reason and faith in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine being critiqued
The target is the philosophical claim that 'the spheres are among those that do not accept generation and corruption' (الأفلاك عند الفلاسفة مما لا يقبل الكون والفساد).
This is the conclusion of the syllogism reconstructed in the previous lesson.
2
Identify the grounds of the critique
The commentator grounds the critique in revelation: 'the Book has spoken of the tearing of the sky on the Day of Resurrection' (وقد نطق الكتاب بخرق السماء يوم القيامة). He also cites the present state: 'today there is no crack in it' (وأما اليوم، فما لها من فطور).
The critique is not based on logical error in the syllogism but on a higher epistemic authority.
3
Explain the commentator's alternative position
The commentator states: 'According to us, the people of Islam, every body, whether sphere or element, is subject to generation and corruption and tearing and repair.' This is a universal affirmative claim (A proposition) that directly contradicts the philosophers' conclusion.
The alternative is not argued syllogistically but asserted based on scriptural evidence.
4
Assess the internal validity of the critique
The critique does not attempt to refute the logical structure of the syllogism; instead, it rejects the major premise by providing a counterexample from revelation. This is a valid move within an Islamic epistemological framework where revelation is a source of certain knowledge that can override fallible human reasoning.
One could argue that the critique is not a logical refutation but a declaration of faith-based authority, which is itself logically valid in a system that accepts revelation as a premise.

فصل في أن الفلك لا يقبل الكون والفساد والخرق والالتيام أما أنه لا يقبل الكون والفساد فلأنه محدد الجهات، ولا شيء من محدد الجهات يقبل الكون والفساد، أما الصغرى، فقد مر تقريرها، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل ما يقبل الكون والفساد فلصورته الحادثة حيز طبعي ولصورته الفاسدة حيز آخر طبعي؛ لما بينا أن كل جسم فله حيز طبعي، وكل ما هذا شأنه فهو قابل للحركة المستقيمة؛ لأن الصورة لا يقبل إلخ اعلم أن من الأجسام ما يقبل الكون والفساد كالعناصر، وسيجيء في العنصريات أن العناصر بعضها ينقلب بعضا بأن يفسد صورته ويحدث صورة أخرى، ومنها ما لا يقبل الكون والفساد والأفلاك عند الفلاسفة مما لا يقبل الكون والفساد وعندنا أهل الإسلام كل جسم سواء كان فلكا أو عنصرا قابل للكون والفساد والخرق والالتيام وقد نطق الكتاب بخرق السماء يوم القيامة، وأما اليوم، فما لها من فطور كما دل عليه الكتاب أيضا. فله حيز لأن الحيز الطبعي ما يقتضيه صورته النوعية ولما كانت الصورتان مختلفتين، كان حيزاهما مختلفين أيضا؛ لامتناع اجتماع جسمين في حيز واحد، وإلا لزم التداخل. هذا شأنه كل جسم يتوارد عليه الصورتان فذلك الجسم قابل للحركة المستقيمة؛ لأن الصورة الكائنة لا تخلو إما أن تكون في حيز طبعي أو في حيز غريب أي غير طبعي، فإن كانت في حيز غريب كانت تقتضي أن تذهب بالحركة المستقيمة إلى حيزها الطبعي؛ لأن الحركة المستديرة لا تقتضي بقاء الجسم في ذلك الحيز، فيلزم كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة وهو باطل، وإن كانت في حيز طبعي فالصورة الفاسدة كانت حين وجودها في حيز غريب فكانت تقتضي حين وجودها أن تخرج ذلك الحيز الغريب وتروح إلى الحيز الطبعي وما ذلك إلا بالحركة المستقيمة، فيلزم أيضا كون الفلك قابلا للحركة المستقيمة، وهو كما ترى.

Two Methods of Proving Rest: Critique of a Conflation
Author describes two methods for proving rest and criticizes conflating them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two methods for proving rest between opposite motions and identify the logical flaw in conflating them.

Core Idea
Conflating distinct logical arguments leads to unnecessary and flawed reasoning.

This passage exposes a common error: mixing two different proofs for the existence of rest at the turning point of a body's motion. The first proof relies on instantaneous arrival and departure; the second on opposing tendencies. The author criticizes a conflation of these two, showing that the second proof is self-sufficient. By understanding this critique, you will sharpen your ability to detect when arguments are improperly combined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
The author names two ways (maslakayn) to prove rest between opposite straight motions. Locate the description of each: the well-known method based on instantaneous arrival/departure, and the tendency method based on opposing inclinations (maylayn).
2
Analyze the first method's reductio argument
The first method argues that arrival at and departure from a limit must be instantaneous (āni). It uses a reductio (khulf): if arrival were temporal and divisible, the body would not be fully arrived at a given instant, contradicting the assumption. Understand the structure of this contradiction.
3
Identify the flaw in the first method
The author points out that the first method leads to infinite rests if applied to intermediate limits assumed in the middle of the distance. Recognize why this infinite regress is problematic: it posits an arbitrary number of rest intervals.
4
Understand the second method (opposing tendencies)
The second method, attributed to the Sheikh (Ibn Sina), bases the proof on the coexistence of two opposing tendencies (mayl) at the point of reversal. It does not rely on instantaneous events or indivisible instants. Grasp how this avoids the infinite regress.
5
Grasp the author's critique of the conflation
The author criticizes the conflation because the tendency method is complete on its own; mixing it with the well-known method is unnecessary and indicates a misunderstanding. Reflect on why the author considers this a logical error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن في إثبات السكون بين الحركتين المستقيمتين مسلكين، الأول: المشهور بين الجمهور أن الوصول إلى الحد حادث في آن لا في زمان؛ لأن الحد عبارة عن منتهى المسافة، فلو كان الوصول زمانيا وانقسم، فحين ما يكون الجسم في أحد الجزئين لم يكن واصلا إلى المنتهى، وقد كان الكلام في الوصول إلى المنتهى، هذا خلف، وكذا اللاوصول أعني ترك ذلك الحد أيضا آني؛ لأنه لو كان زمانيا وترك الحد شيئا فشيئا، فحين ما يكون في أحد الجزئين لم يكن تاركا للحد، وقد فرضناه تاركا للحد، هذا خلف فإذا كان الوصول واللاوصول آنيا ولم يتصور اجتماعهما في آن واحد كان في آنين متغايرين، وإذا كان تتالي الآنين باطلا؛ لاستلزامه جزءا لا يتجزى كان بينهما زمان وذلك الزمان زمان السكون لانتفاء الحركة، أما الذاهبة فقد انتفت حين الوصول، وأما الراجعة فلحد مشيها بعد ذلك الزمان، وهذا البيان منقوض بالحدود المفروضة في وسط المسافة، فيلزم سكونات غير متناهية، ولم يرض به الشيخ وأقام من عنده برهانا باعتبار الميلين المتنافيين، كما قررناه سابقا، والمصنف له خلط بين المسلكين، ساق الكلام أولا باعتبار الميلين، ثم سلك المسلك المشهور باعتبار كون الوصول واللاوصول آنيا أن مسلك الميلين تام لا حاجة له إلى المسلك المشهور.
Know that in proving rest between two straight motions there are two paths: First, the well-known among the public that arrival at the limit occurs in an instant, not in time, because the limit is the end of the distance. For if the arrival were temporal and divisible, then when the body is in one of the two parts it would not have arrived at the end, while the discussion was about arrival at the end; this is a contradiction. Similarly, non-arrival—i.e., leaving that limit—is also instantaneous; because if it were temporal and it leaves the limit gradually, then when it is in one of the parts it would not have left the limit, whereas we assumed it is leaving the limit; this is a contradiction. So if arrival and non-arrival are instantaneous and it is inconceivable that they coincide in one instant, they are in two different instants. And if the succession of two instants is invalid because it necessitates an indivisible part, then there is time between them, and that time is the time of rest due to absence of motion. The departing motion has ceased at the time of arrival, and the returning motion begins after that time. This demonstration is refuted by the limits assumed in the middle of the distance, leading to infinite rests. The Sheikh was not satisfied with this and established from himself a proof based on the two opposing tendencies, as we stated earlier. The author confused the two paths: he first proceeded with the tendency path, then took the well-known path based on arrival and non-arrival being instantaneous, whereas the tendency path is complete and does not need the well-known path.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a common conflation in proving rest between two opposite straight motions. The author presents two methods. The first method, popular among the public, argues that arrival at a limit and departure from it are instantaneous events. If they were temporal and divisible, the body would not have fully arrived or departed at a given instant, which is contradictory. Since they occur in different instants, and instants cannot be immediately successive without an indivisible part, there must be a time interval between them—this is the time of rest. However, this argument fails because it would require infinite rests at every intermediate limit of the distance. The second method, attributed to the Sheikh (Ibn Sina), uses the concept of two opposing tendencies (mayl) in the body at the point of reversal. The author criticizes the conflation of these two methods: the tendency method is sufficient on its own, and mixing it with the popular method is unnecessary.

Key Terms
مسلك
آن
خلف
ميل
جزء لا يتجزى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It assumes time is composed of indivisible parts
B) It leads to infinite rests at intermediate limits
C) It fails to account for the tendency
D) It is not based on Aristotle

Key Takeaway
The well-known method for proving rest by instantaneous arrival/departure is flawed because it implies infinite rests; the tendency method is a sufficient alternative and should not be conflated with it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a debate about the motion of a projectile, a mutakallim argues that at the peak of its flight, the projectile must be at rest because arrival and departure are instantaneous, and between them there must be time. Identify which method this argument belongs to, and explain the flaw the author highlights.

Momentary Rest vs Temporal Motion in Jadal
Author argues celestial sphere's momentary rest does not obstruct another's temporal motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between momentary and temporal motion/rest in a jadal exchange and evaluate whether mutual obstruction (mumana'a) occurs.

Core Idea
A momentary cessation does not obstruct a temporal motion because they belong to different categories of time.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical argument about the celestial sphere's motion. The key logical move is distinguishing between momentary rest (sukun ani) and temporal motion (haraka zamaniyya). Understanding this distinction is crucial for resolving objections in jadal, especially when dealing with magnitudes and durations. We'll walk through the claims, objections, and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Find the author's initial assertion: the celestial sphere's rest is momentary, so it does not obstruct the motion of another body (the mountain). Note that 'momentary' means at an instant, not over time.
The term 'ani' is key; it means instantaneous.
2
Identify the Objection
The objector raises two points: (1) The sphere moves accidentally by the mountain, later develops inclination to return, violating the rule that rest must occur between two opposite motions. (2) More refined: if the sphere's rest is momentary and the mountain's motion in that instant is also momentary, they directly oppose each other, forcing the sphere to rest.
The objection leverages the idea that at the same instant, opposite attributes (rest vs motion) would conflict.
3
Find the Author's Response
The author responds: (1) Essential rest vs accidental motion is not contradictory, using the ship passenger analogy. (2) Motion cannot exist in an instant because motion is gradual (khuruj min al-quwwa ila al-fi'l 'ala sabill al-tadrij). Thus momentary rest is only a metaphor for cessation at an instant, not a real duration, so no obstruction occurs.
The author denies that motion exists in an instant, so there is no direct momentary opposition.
4
Analyze the Core Distinction
The crux is that momentary rest (instant) and temporal motion (duration) are categorically different: one is a point, the other a line. They cannot directly obstruct because obstruction requires temporal overlap.
This distinction is a standard tool in jadal to resolve apparent contradictions.
5
Evaluate the Argument's Strength
The author's response holds if we accept that motion is not instantaneous. The conditional at the end ('Yes, if it rested for a time...') acknowledges that if the rest were temporal, obstruction would follow. So the argument depends on the definition of 'instant'.
In Islamic physics, motion is always temporal; this is a key premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني في الفلكيات فصل في أن الفلك يتحرك ولكنهم غير مانع الحركة الجبل؛ لأن سكونها آني، وحركة الجبل زمانية وليس بينهما ممانعة. ولكنها متحركة بالعرض بواسطة الجبل في زمان لم يحدث فيه ميل الرجوع فيها، ولا منافاة بين السكون بالذات والتحرك بالعرض كجالس السفينة، فإنه ساكن بالذات ومتحرك بالعرض، ثم بعد زمان يحدث فيها ميل الرجوع فتحرك حينئذ بالذات، فلا تنتقض قاعدة وجوب السكون ولا يلزم سكون الجبل، وأما جواب المصنف محليه فليس بصاف؛ لأن بناء الإشكال على وجوب السكون زمانا بين حركتين، ولا شك أن بين وجوب السكون للحبة زمانا وبين حركة الجبل في ذلك الزمان ممانعة ظاهرة، إلا أن يقال : سكونها بالذات زمانا لا ينافي تحركها بالعرض في ذلك الزمان فلا يمانع حركة الجبل، فحينئذ هذا الجواب إلى ما قلنا من قبل، ويلغو ذكر يرجع سكون اني للحبة وعدم تنافيه تحركة زمانية للجبل. سكونها : فإن قيل: إن بإزاء سكون الحبة الآني حركة الجبل الآنية، فيكون بين سكونها الآني وحركته الآنية ممانعة، فيلزم سكون الجبل، فما يغني هذا الجواب، قلت: لا يتصور وجود الحركة في الآن؛ لأن معناه الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج، وكذلك السكون المقابل للحركة ولذا لا يقف الجسم في الآن بالحركة والسكون وأما قوله : "سكونها آني" فمعناه انقطاع الحركة حين التلاقي، وسماه مجازا بالسكون. :آني حادث في آن ،الملاقاة، ولا يستمر سكونها زمانا حتى يلزم سكون الجبل، بل تتحرك بالعرض بواسطة الجبل. نعم لو سكنت زمانا و لم تتحرك بالعرض في زمان حركة الجبل، لزم سكون الجبل.
The second art on celestial matters. Section: That the celestial sphere moves. But it does not obstruct the motion of the mountain [body]; because its rest is momentary, and the motion of the mountain is temporal, and there is no mutual obstruction between them. But it moves accidentally by means of the mountain in a time in which no inclination to return occurs in it, and there is no contradiction between essential rest and accidental motion, like the passenger of a ship, for he is essentially at rest and accidentally moving. Then, after a time, an inclination to return occurs in it, so it then moves essentially, so the rule of necessary rest is not violated, nor does the rest of the mountain follow. As for the author's [؟] answer, it is not clear, because the problem is built on the necessity of rest for a time between two motions, and there is no doubt that between the necessity of rest for the grain [for a time] and the motion of the mountain in that time there is an apparent mutual obstruction. Except if it is said: its essential rest for a time does not negate its accidental motion in that time, so it does not obstruct the mountain's motion. Then this answer is what we said before, and the mention of the grain's momentary rest and its non-contradiction with the mountain's temporal motion becomes futile. Its rest: For if it is said: Opposite the grain's momentary rest is the mountain's momentary motion, so there is mutual obstruction between its momentary rest and its momentary motion, so the rest of the mountain follows. What does this answer avail? I say: The existence of motion in an instant is inconceivable, because its meaning is exiting from potentiality to actuality gradually, and likewise rest opposite to motion; therefore, the body is not in an instant with motion and rest. And as for his saying: 'Its rest is momentary,' its meaning is the cessation of motion at the time of meeting, and he called it rest metaphorically. It occurs in an instant at the moment of meeting, and its rest does not endure for a time such that the rest of the mountain would follow, but rather it moves accidentally by means of the mountain. Yes, if it rested for a time and did not move accidentally during the time of the mountain's motion, then the rest of the mountain would follow.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange concerning the motion of the celestial sphere and whether its rest obstructs the motion of another body (here termed 'al-jabal', likely meaning a heavy body or the earth). The author begins by stating that the celestial sphere's rest is momentary (ani), while the motion of the other body is temporal (zamaniyya), so there is no mutual obstruction. An objector then raises a problem: the celestial sphere moves accidentally (bil-'arad) by means of the other body's motion, but later develops an inclination to return, moving essentially. This, the objector claims, violates the rule that rest must occur between two opposing motions. The author responds by distinguishing essential rest from accidental motion, using the analogy of a passenger on a ship who is essentially at rest but accidentally moving. The discussion continues with a refined objection concerning a grain (al-habba) that rests momentarily while the mountain moves momentarily, suggesting a direct contradiction. The answer clarifies that motion and rest in an instant are inconceivable because motion requires gradual change; thus, momentary rest is only a metaphor for the cessation of motion at an instant, not a true quantitative rest. Therefore, the celestial sphere's momentary 'rest' does not obstruct the other body's motion. All examples are from natural philosophy; no fiqh or kalam examples here, but the analytical method is typical of jadal.

Key Terms
سكون آني
حركة زمانية
ممانعة

A) Because momentary rest is stronger than temporal motion.
B) Because momentary rest and temporal motion are categorically different (instant vs duration).
C) Because the mountain is moving accidentally.
D) Because the celestial sphere's rest is essential.

Key Takeaway
Momentary rest and temporal motion belong to different time categories, so they do not mutually obstruct; this is a key logical distinction in jadal and kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In a jadal session, the claimant says: 'The celestial sphere's rest is momentary, so it does not obstruct the earth's motion.' As the objector, formulate the refined objection from the passage (momentary rest vs momentary motion). Then, as the respondent, provide the author's answer that motion is not instantaneous.
?

Does the eternal power obstruct the temporal effect? Use the logic of momentary vs temporal categories to argue.

Distinguishing Qualities from Substantial Forms via Conditional Argument
Author argues that sensible qualities are distinct from substantial forms via a conditional proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the conditional argument that the primary sensible qualities are additional to the substantial form.

Core Idea
If qualities were the form, changing qualities would change the substance – but it doesn't.

In this passage, the author presents a straightforward proof using a conditional inference: if the sensible qualities were identical to the substantial form, then when the quality changes, the substance would have to change. Since we observe that water remains water when heated – only losing its coldness – the qualities must be additional to the form. This logical move is essential for understanding the distinction between essential and accidental attributes in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if' clause: 'If the qualities were the same as the natural forms...' This is the antecedent of the conditional argument.
The passage states: 'ولو كانت نفس الصور الطبعية لاستحال ذلك' – 'If they were the same as the natural forms, that would be impossible.'
2
Identify the observed contrary evidence
The author provides empirical examples: heated water remains water, cooled air remains air. The coldness of water disappears but the water nature persists.
These examples are the factual basis for denying the consequent of the conditional.
3
Apply modus tollens
The argument follows modus tollens: If P (qualities = form), then Q (change in quality would change form). Not Q (form remains despite quality change). Therefore, not P (qualities are not identical to form).
This is a valid conditional inference.
4
State the conclusion
Since the consequent is false (form survives quality change), the antecedent must be false. Hence the qualities are additional (zāʾida) to the form.
The conclusion directly supports the doctrine that primary qualities are accidents, not essential parts of the element.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول أيضا: الكيفيات زائدة على الصورة الطبعية؛ لأنها تستحيل في الكيفيات مثل التسخن والتبرد مع بقاء الصور الطبعية بذواتها، ولو كانت نفس الصور الطبعية لاستحال ذلك. الكيفيات إلخ :حاصله أن هذه الكيفية المحسوسة من الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة مغايرة لطبائع العناصر؛ لأن هذه الكيفيات تزول عن العناصر حقائقها النوعية كالماء المتسخن والهواء المتبرد قد زالت برودة الماء مع بقاء طبيعة، وكذا الهواء زالت حرارته مع بقاء صورتها النوعية، ولو كانت هذه الكيفيات عين صورها لم يكن بقاء الصور مع زوال هذه الكيفيات.
We also say: The qualities are additional to the natural form, because they undergo change in the qualities such as heating and cooling while the natural forms remain in their essences. If they were the same as the natural forms, that would be impossible. The qualities, etc.: Its summary is that these sensible qualities of heat, cold, wetness, and dryness are distinct from the natures of the elements, because these qualities depart from the elements while their specific realities remain, like heated water and cooled air: the coldness of water has gone while its nature remains, and likewise the heat of air has gone while its specific form remains. If these qualities were identical to their forms, the forms would not persist when these qualities disappear.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the primary qualities (heat, cold, wetness, dryness) are accidental to the elements and not identical to their substantial forms. The author argues: qualities like heating and cooling can change while the substance remains the same (e.g., water can be heated but remains water). If the quality were the same as the substantial form, then changing the quality would change the substance, which is not the case. Therefore, the qualities are additional (zāʾida) to the natural form. The commentary explains this further: water can lose its coldness and become hot, yet it remains water; air can lose its heat and become cold, yet it remains air. This shows that the qualities are not essential.

Key Terms
الصورة الطبعية (al-ṣūra al-ṭabīʿiyya): The natural form or substantial form of an element, which remains despite changes in qualities.
الكيفيات (al-kayfiyyāt): The sensible qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry) that are accidental to the elements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It becomes fire
B) It ceases to be water
C) It remains water while losing coldness
D) It becomes air

Key Takeaway
The essential logical point is that by observing the persistence of substances under qualitative change, we can prove that qualities are accidental to the form through a valid modus tollens argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar conditional argument using modus tollens to show that the quality of 'sweetness' is additional to the nature of honey. Use the observation that honey can become sour (e.g., when fermented) while still being called honey in some sense (or consider a more precise example from Islamic science).
?
Take an example from Islamic medicine: the quality of 'heat' in the body changes due to fever, yet the human nature (al-ṭabīʿa) remains. Formulate a conditional argument proving that bodily heat is accidental, not identical to the human nature.

Defining Mizaj: Genus and Differentia
Author defines mizaj as the intermediate quality from mixture of elemental simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of mizaj and classify the definition type.

Core Idea
Mizaj is the balanced quality resulting from mutual tempering of opposing elemental qualities.

In this passage, the author defines mizaj (temperament) as the intermediate quality that arises when the four simple elemental bodies combine and interact. This definition is foundational in Islamic natural philosophy and medicine. We will analyze its logical structure by extracting the genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage. It is stated explicitly at the end of the first sentence: 'wa huwa al-mizaj'.
The definiendum is المزاج (mizaj).
2
Extract the genus
Look for the broader category under which mizaj falls. The passage says: 'fatahsalu kayfiyya mutawassita' (there results an intermediate quality). The genus is 'kayfiyya' (quality), specifically 'kayfiyya mutawassita' (intermediate quality).
Genus is a type of quality.
3
Extract the differentia
Identify what distinguishes mizaj from other intermediate qualities. The passage specifies: 'min imtizaj al-basa'it wa takasur kull wahid minha surat kayfiyyat al-akhar' (from the mixture of the simples and the breaking of each one's intensity). So the differentia is the process of mutual tempering from elemental mixtures.
Differentia involves causation by mixture of opposites.
4
Classify the definition type
Determine if this is a true definition (hadd haqiqi) or a description (rasm). Since it describes how mizaj arises rather than its essence (it is a quality, not a substance), and it includes a cause, it is likely a rasm (descriptive definition).
The commentator later clarifies additional nuance.
5
Test completeness
Check if the definition covers all instances of mizaj and excludes non-instances. Does every intermediate quality from elemental mixture count as mizaj? Yes. Does it exclude qualities not from such mixture? Yes.
The definition is complete for the intended scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبسائط إذا اجتمعت في المركب وفعل بعضها في بعض بقواها، وكسر كل واحد منها سورة كيفية الآخر، فتحصل كيفية متوسطة توسطا ما بين الكيفيات المتضادة متشابهة في أجزائه، وهو المزاج. يعني البسائط : هذا شروع في كيفية حدوث المركبات من البسائط العنصرية، حاصله: أن البسائط العنصرية إذا اجتمعت في حيز واحد بسبب من الأسباب وتماست، وأثر كل واحد منها بقواها المتضادة في الآخر، وكسر كل واحد منها حدة كيفية الآخر، كسرت البرودة حدة الحرارة ،وبالعكس، وكذلك الرطوبة واليبوسة، فتحصل بعد الكسر والانكسار كيفية معتدلة اعتدالا ما بين تلك الكيفيات المتضادة، متشابهة في جميع أجزاء المركب وتسمى تلك الكيفيات بالمزاج، وهو مصدر من المفاعلة على وزن قتال، وسميت به لحصولها بسبب الممازجة والاجتماع، ولا تظنن أن الكيفية الواحدة الشخصية قائمة بجميع العناصر ؛ لأن قيام الكيفية الشخصية بمحال متعددة باطل ضرورة أن تشخص الحال تابع لتشخص المحل والمحل ههنا متعدد بالشخص، فالحال أيضا كذلك، بل المراد أن الكيفية القائمة بالجزء الناري المعتدلة بين الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة مماثلة للكيفية القائمة بالجزء المائي المعتدلة بين تلك الكيفيات، وقس عليه الجزء الهوائي والأرضي.
And when the simple bodies come together in the composite and act upon each other through their powers, each one breaking the intensity of the quality of the other, there results an intermediate quality, a mean between the opposing qualities, similar in its parts, and that is the temperament. Meaning: 'the simple bodies': This is a start on how composites arise from elemental simple bodies. Its summary: When elemental simple bodies come together in a single locus due to some cause and touch each other, and each one affects the other through its opposing powers, each one breaking the intensity of the quality of the other—cold breaks the intensity of heat, and vice versa, and similarly moisture and dryness—then after breaking and being broken, a balanced quality results, a balance between those opposing qualities, similar in all parts of the composite, and that quality is called 'temperament'. It is a verbal noun from the mutual form on the pattern of 'qital', and it is so named because it occurs due to mingling and coming together. Do not think that a single specific quality subsists in all the elements; for the subsistence of a specific quality in multiple loci is false, necessarily because the individuation of the state follows the individuation of the locus, and the locus here is multiple in individuality, so the state is likewise. Rather, what is meant is that the quality subsisting in the fiery part, balanced between heat and cold and moisture and dryness, is similar to the quality subsisting in the watery part, balanced between those qualities, and analogize to that the airy and earthy parts.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines mizaj (temperament) and then expands on the definition through a commentator's explanation. The original text states: when simple elemental bodies combine in a composite and act on each other with their opposing powers, each tempers the other's quality, resulting in an intermediate quality that is uniform throughout the composite—this is mizaj. The commentator then explains this process: elemental simples (fire, air, water, earth) come together in one place, touch, and each affects the other with its qualities (heat/cold, moisture/dryness). The extremes break each other's intensity, yielding a balanced quality between the extremes, uniform throughout the composite. This balanced quality is called mizaj, a term derived from 'mumazaja' (mingling). The commentator warns against a misunderstanding: do not think there is one single subjective quality that inheres in all elements simultaneously, because a subjective quality cannot subsist in multiple distinct loci; rather, each element's part has its own balanced quality that is similar to the others, but not numerically identical. For example, the quality in the fiery part (balanced between heat and cold, moisture and dryness) is similar to that in the watery part, and likewise for airy and earthy parts.

Key Terms
البسائط
المزاج
الكيفية المتوسطة
الامتزاج

B) An intermediate quality
C) The mutual breaking of intensities
D) The composite body

Key Takeaway
The definition of mizaj combines the genus 'intermediate quality' with the differentia 'resulting from the mutual tempering of elemental simples', making it a descriptive definition (rasm).

The Logical Refinement: Mizaj as Similar, Not Identical
Commentator warns against misunderstanding that mizaj is a single quality across elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentator's logical argument that mizaj is not a single subjective quality but rather similar qualities in each part, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
A single quality cannot subsist in multiple loci; mizaj is similarity across parts, not numerical identity.

After defining mizaj, the commentator anticipates a common misunderstanding: that the intermediate quality is a single subjective quality present in all elemental parts simultaneously. He provides a logical proof based on the principle that the individuation of a state follows the individuation of its locus. Let's analyze this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The commentator says: 'Do not think that a single specific quality subsists in all the elements.' This is the misunderstanding he aims to correct.
The claim would be that a single, numerically identical quality exists in each elemental part.
2
Extract the reason given
The commentator provides a reason: 'because the subsistence of a specific quality in multiple loci is false, necessarily because the individuation of the state follows the individuation of the locus, and the locus here is multiple in individuality.'
The key principle: the identity of a state is tied to the identity of its locus.
3
Reconstruct the argument as a syllogism
Major premise: For any state (quality), its individuation follows the individuation of its locus. Minor premise: The elemental parts in a composite are multiple and distinct loci. Conclusion: Therefore, the qualities in each part are multiple and distinct, not one single quality.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure? Actually, it is a deductive argument using a universal principle.
4
Evaluate the validity of the argument
The argument is logically valid: if the premises are true, the conclusion necessarily follows. The principle that a state's identity depends on its locus is widely accepted in Islamic philosophy.
The argument is sound if the premises are accepted.
5
Apply the principle to another Islamic example
The same principle is used in Kalam to discuss divine attributes: if knowledge and power were identical to the essence, they would not be multiple; but the effects are multiple, so attributes are distinct (according to Ash'aris). However, some argue that attributes are not loci.
This shows the broad applicability of the state-locus principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبسائط إذا اجتمعت في المركب وفعل بعضها في بعض بقواها، وكسر كل واحد منها سورة كيفية الآخر، فتحصل كيفية متوسطة توسطا ما بين الكيفيات المتضادة متشابهة في أجزائه، وهو المزاج. يعني البسائط : هذا شروع في كيفية حدوث المركبات من البسائط العنصرية، حاصله: أن البسائط العنصرية إذا اجتمعت في حيز واحد بسبب من الأسباب وتماست، وأثر كل واحد منها بقواها المتضادة في الآخر، وكسر كل واحد منها حدة كيفية الآخر، كسرت البرودة حدة الحرارة ،وبالعكس، وكذلك الرطوبة واليبوسة، فتحصل بعد الكسر والانكسار كيفية معتدلة اعتدالا ما بين تلك الكيفيات المتضادة، متشابهة في جميع أجزاء المركب وتسمى تلك الكيفيات بالمزاج، وهو مصدر من المفاعلة على وزن قتال، وسميت به لحصولها بسبب الممازجة والاجتماع، ولا تظنن أن الكيفية الواحدة الشخصية قائمة بجميع العناصر ؛ لأن قيام الكيفية الشخصية بمحال متعددة باطل ضرورة أن تشخص الحال تابع لتشخص المحل والمحل ههنا متعدد بالشخص، فالحال أيضا كذلك، بل المراد أن الكيفية القائمة بالجزء الناري المعتدلة بين الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة مماثلة للكيفية القائمة بالجزء المائي المعتدلة بين تلك الكيفيات، وقس عليه الجزء الهوائي والأرضي.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the commentator's argument in your own words as a categorical syllogism. Identify the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion. Then check if the argument would still hold if the loci were not multiple (e.g., if the composite were homogeneous).
?
Apply the state-locus principle to an example from Islamic theology: If God's essence is one, but He has many attributes (knowledge, power, will), can a single attribute subsist in multiple created effects? Use the principle to argue either for or against.

Causal Explanation of Atmospheric Phenomena
Author explains the formation of clouds, rain, snow, hail, dew, and frost based on vapor and air layers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the causal chain from vapor to each atmospheric phenomenon (rain, snow, hail, dew, frost) based on the conditions of cold and condensation described in the passage.

Core Idea
Rain, snow, hail, dew, and frost are variations of vapor condensation under different degrees and timing of cold.

This passage presents a causal account of atmospheric phenomena rooted in the classical theory of four elements and atmospheric layers. By understanding how vapor interacts with heat and cold, you will see how the same basic process—condensation—produces different outcomes depending on the intensity and timing of cold. This lesson will guide you through that causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting material
The passage begins by describing vapor (al-bukhār) as watery parts mixed with airy parts, made light by heat, rising from water bodies. This is the raw material for all phenomena.
2
Recognize the role of cold layers
The atmosphere has four layers: two hot, one very cold (Zamhariri), and one cold near water. When vapor reaches the cold layers, it condenses due to cold.
The Zamhariri layer is the key cold layer.
3
Distinguish outcomes based on cold intensity and timing
Moderate cold leads to cloud and rain. Strong cold before vapor gathers into cloud yields snow; strong cold after gathering yields hail. If vapor is sparse and stays near ground, night cold gives dew (non-frozen) or frost (frozen).
Also note the exceptional case of direct air condensation.
4
Consider empirical support
The author cites observations by 'the Sheikh' and a trustworthy person to support the theory, showing that causal reasoning can be grounded in empirical evidence.
This reflects the epistemological method of the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأكثري: إنما قال: السبب الأكثري؛ لأن المطر قد يتكون من تكاثف الهواء وصيرورته ماء متقاطرا، كما قال من قبل في بحث انقلاب العناصر بعضها إلى بعض البخار أجزاء مائية مخالطة بأجزاء هوائية تلطفت بالحرارة، وبتلك الحرارة مالت بالقسر إلى ،فوق، ولما كان في الجو هواء وهو حار كاد يذهب الوهم، ويخطر بالبال ما بال بخار تكاثف هناك، وليس هناك برودة توجبه؟ فقال المصنف معللا بقوله: "لأن ما يجاور الماء" إلخ. قالت الحكماء : إن للهواء أربع طبقات: الطبقة الأولى والثانية . **  الفن الثالث في العنصريات ". فصل في كائنات الجو أجزاء البخار الصاعد؛ لأن ما يجاور الماء من الهواء يستفيد كيفية البرد من الماء، ثم الطبقة التي ينقطع عنها تأثير شعاع الشمس تبقى باردة، فإذا بلغ البخار في صعوده إليها تكاثف بواسطة البرد، فإن لم يكن البرد قويا اجتمع ذلك وتقاطر، فالمجتمع هو السحاب والمتقاطر هو المطر، وإن كان البرد قويا فإما أن يصل البرد إلى أجزاء السحاب قبل اجتماعها أو لا يصل، فإن وصل قبل اجتماعها ينزل السحاب ثلجا، وإن لم يصل ينزل بردا، وأما إذا لم يصل إلى الطبقة الباردة فإن كان من - لقرب النار حارتان يحدث فيهما النيازك والشهب والثالثة - وهي سميت زمهريرية والرابعة لقرب الماء باردتان والثالثة بقيت على كيفية البرد التي استفادتها الماء؛ لعدم وصول تأثر عكس شعاع الشمس إليها، والرابعة لم تبق على كيفية البرد المستفادة من الماء؛ لتأثير شعاع الشمس، فإذا بلغ البخار إليها تكاثف بسبب البرد الذي هناك، فإن لم يكن البرد قويا اجتمع ذلك البخار وتقاطر، فالمجتمع هو السحاب، والمتقاطر هو المطر، وإن كان البرد قويا ووصل إلى أجزاء السحاب- وهو البخار قبل اجتماعها - ينزل ثلجا، وإن وصل بعد اجتماعها ينزل بردا، وإن لم يصل البخار إلى الطبقة الزمهريرة لقلة الحرارة، فإن كان كثيرا فقد يحدث منه سحاب ماطر، وحكى الشيخ أنه شاهد البخار قد صعد من السافل بعض الجبال صعودا يسيرا، كأنه مكب موضوعة على وهدة، وكان هو فوق تلك الغمامة في الشمس، أهل القرية التي كانت هناك يمطرون وحكى لي ثقة أنه شاهد من تحته وكان من مثل ذلك في بعض الجبال الشمالية. وقد لا يحدث منه سحاب، بل يبقى شبيها بالدخان متصلا بالأرض، وبارتفاع الشمس ينجلي وإن كان البخار قليلا، فإذا ضربه برد الليل ولم ينحمد فهو الطل، وإن انجمد فهو الصقيع.
The primary [cause]: He only said 'the primary cause' because rain may also be formed from condensation of air and its turning into dripping water, as he said previously in the discussion of the transformation of elements into each other: vapor is watery parts mixed with airy parts, made subtle by heat, and by that heat they were forcibly inclined upward. And when there is air in the atmosphere which is hot, the imagination almost goes away, and it occurs to the mind: what about vapor that condenses there when there is no cold to necessitate it? So the author said, giving the reason, 'because what is adjacent to water' etc. The philosophers said: Air has four layers: the first layer and the second. [Page 61] The third art on elemental things. Chapter on atmospheric phenomena: The parts of rising vapor are such because what is adjacent to water from the air acquires the quality of cold from the water, then the layer which is cut off from the effect of the sun's ray remains cold. So when the vapor in its ascent reaches it, it condenses by means of the cold. If the cold is not strong, it gathers and drips: what gathers is cloud, and what drips is rain. If the cold is strong, then either the cold reaches the parts of the cloud before they gather or it does not. If it reaches before their gathering, the cloud descends as snow; if it does not reach, it descends as hail. And if it does not reach the cold layer, then if it is from—because of proximity to fire, two hot layers in which meteors and shooting stars occur, and the third—which is called Zamhariri (very cold), and the fourth, due to proximity to water, are two cold layers. The third remains on the cold quality it acquired from water because the influence of the reflected sun ray does not reach it, and the fourth does not remain on the cold quality acquired from water because of the effect of the sun's ray. So when the vapor reaches it, it condenses due to the cold there. If the cold is not strong, that vapor gathers and drips: what gathers is cloud, and what drips is rain. If the cold is strong and reaches the parts of the cloud—that is, the vapor before its gathering—it descends as snow; and if it reaches after its gathering, it descends as hail. If the vapor does not reach the Zamhariri layer due to lack of heat, then if it is abundant, a rain cloud may form from it. The Sheikh narrated that he saw vapor rising from the lower part of some mountains a small ascent, as if it were an inverted pot placed on a depression, and he was above that cloud in the sun, and the people of the village that was there were being rained upon. A trustworthy person narrated to me that he saw from beneath it, and similar things occurred in some northern mountains. Sometimes no cloud forms from it, but it remains like smoke attached to the ground, and with the rise of the sun it clears. If the vapor is little, then if the cold of night strikes it and it does not congeal, it is dew; and if it congeals, it is frost.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage explains the causes of various atmospheric phenomena including rain, snow, hail, dew, and frost. It begins with a commentator's note justifying why the author called condensation the 'primary cause' (al-sabab al-akthari) of rain, since rain might also form from direct condensation of air (without vapor), but that is less common. The main explanation relies on the theory of four atmospheric layers: two hot layers near fire (where meteors occur), a very cold layer (Zamhariri) in the middle, and a cold layer near water. The vapor rises from water bodies, and when it reaches the cold layers, it condenses. If the cold is moderate, the vapor forms clouds and then rain. If the cold is strong and reaches the vapor before it gathers into cloud, snow falls; if after gathering, hail falls. If the vapor does not reach the cold layers due to insufficient heat, but is abundant, it can still form a rain cloud (as observed by 'the Sheikh' near mountains). If vapor is sparse and remains near the ground, it becomes dew if struck by night cold without freezing, or frost if it freezes. The passage thus provides a causal explanation grounded in the properties of the four elements and their interactions with heat and cold.

Key Takeaway
All atmospheric precipitation phenomena follow from the same process of vapor condensation, differentiated by the intensity and timing of cold.
