The First Proof: The Dilemma of Division
First argument proving soul's immateriality via dilemma of intelligible forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum argument that the soul cannot be material because intelligible forms would have to be both divisible and indivisible.

Core Idea
If the soul were material, its intelligible forms would have to be both divisible and indivisible—an impossibility.

The author presents a powerful dilemma. Assuming the soul is material (divisible), the intelligible forms it contains must be either simple or composite. Both possibilities lead to a contradiction, proving the assumption false. This lesson walks you through each step of this logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption to be refuted
The argument begins by assuming the opposite: that the soul is material and therefore divisible. This is the hypothesis that leads to contradiction.
In logic, this is called reductio ad absurdum (khalf).
2
Recognize the two possibilities for intelligible forms
If the soul is divisible, then the intelligible forms inhering in it must be either simple (indivisible) or composite (made of parts). There is no third option.
This covers all possibilities, forming a dilemma.
3
Analyze the case of simple forms
If the form is simple, it is indivisible. However, because it inheres in a divisible subject (the soul), it would become divided—the state in one part differs from another. This contradicts its simplicity.
The simple cannot be divided, yet division follows from the divisible subject.
4
Analyze the case of composite forms
If the form is composite, it must resolve into simple parts (otherwise infinite regress). Those simple parts also inhere in the divisible soul, so they become divided—again a contradiction.
The regress argument ensures we reach simples that then become divided.
5
Conclude the contradiction
Both possibilities lead to an absurdity (khalf). Therefore, the original assumption (soul is material) is false. The soul must be immaterial (mujarrada).
This is the desired conclusion that the soul is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإنسان
ذات ،وضع، فإما أن لا تنقسم أو تنقسم، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن كل ما له وضع ينقسم، على ما مر في نفي الجزء، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن معقولاتها إن كانت بسيطة يلزم انقسامها؛ لأن الحال في أحد جزئيها غير الحال في الجزء الآخر، وإن كانت مركبة، وكل مركب إنما يتركب من البسائط، فيلزم انقسام تلك البسائط، هذا خلف، ونقول أيضا: إن التعقل ليس بالآلة الجسمانية، وإلا يعرض لها الكلال لضعف البدن،
ذات وضع أي قابلة للإشارة الحسية ضرورة أن المادة ذات وضع، فالقائم بها أيضا يكون ذا وضع معقولاتها حاصله أن النفس إن كانت مادية منقسمة وصور المعقولات حالة فيها، فلا تخلو تلك الصور من أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة، فإن كانت بسيطة لزم انقسام البسيط؛ لأن الوصف الحال في محل منقسم ينقسم لا محالة بواسطة، والنفس على هذا التقدير منقسمة، فالصورة الحالة فيها أيضا منقسمة، وقد كانت بسيطة، هذا خلف. وإن كانت مركبة فكل مركب ينتهي تركيبه إلى بسيط، وإلا لزم تركيبه من أجزاء غير متناهية وذلك باطل بالتطبيق، فذلك البسيط المنتهي إليه المركب حال في النفس أيضا، فيلزم انقسامه بواسطة النفس، وهذا خلف، فلما لزم الخلف على تقدير كون النفس مادية على كلا الشقين بطل كونها مادية، فثبت كونها مجردة، وهو المطلوب. ونقول إلخ: دليل آخر على تجرد النفس، ويمكن أن يكون المراد به أن القوة العاقلة ليست جسمانية، بل صفة قائمة بالنفس وإلا إلخ أي لو كان التعقل بالآلة الجسمانية يعرض لها الكلال عند ضعف البدن؛ لأن القوى الجسمانية قائمة بالبدن، فلما ضعف البدن ضعفت القوى البدنية، كما ترى الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة تكل بعد الأربعين، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة للنفس مادية لكلت أيضا بعد الأربعين كسائر القوى الجسمانية، والتالي باطل؛ لأن القوة العاقلة بعد الأربعين قوية على حالها، بل - وليس كذلك؛ لأن البدن بعد الأربعين يأخذ في النقصان مع أن القوة العاقلة هناك تشرع في الكمال، - تزيد قوة فلما كانت القوة العاقلة غير جسمانية كانت النفس أيضا غير جسمانية؛ ضرورة أن تجرد الوصف يستلزم تجرد الموصوف. الكمال: فإن الإدراك والعقل بعد الأربعين كامل وكمال العلم يدل على كمال القوة العاقلة، ولو كانت جسمانية لضعفت.
Section on the human. [The soul is] a substance possessing position, so either it is not divisible or it is divisible. The first is impossible because everything with position is divisible, as previously mentioned in refuting the indivisible part. The second is also impossible because its intelligibles, if they are simple, would entail their division, since the state in one part differs from the state in another part; and if they are composite, every composite is only composed of simples, so the division of those simples is entailed—this is a contradiction. We also say: intellection is not through a bodily organ, for then fatigue would occur due to bodily weakness. [Commentary:] 'possessing position' means capable of sensory indication, for matter necessarily has position, so what subsists in it also has position. The meaning of 'its intelligibles' is that if the soul were material and divisible, and the forms of intelligibles inhere in it, then those forms are either simple or composite. If simple, the simple becomes divisible because a description inhering in a divisible locus is necessarily divided through it, and on this assumption the soul is divisible, so the form inhering in it is also divisible, yet it was simple—a contradiction. If composite, every composite resolves into simples, otherwise it would be composed of infinite parts, which is invalid by the method of superimposition, so that ultimate simple in the composite inheres in the soul as well, entailing its division through the soul—a contradiction. Since a contradiction follows on both horns of assuming the soul is material, its materiality is refuted, and its immateriality is established, which is the desired conclusion. 'We also say' etc.: another proof of the soul's immateriality, possibly meaning that the rational faculty is not corporeal but an attribute subsisting in the soul; otherwise, if intellection were through a bodily organ, fatigue would affect it when the body weakens. Since bodily powers subsist in the body, when the body weakens the bodily powers weaken, as we see the external and internal senses tire after forty. So if the rational faculty of the soul were material, it would also tire after forty like other bodily powers. The consequent is false because the rational faculty after forty is strong as it is—rather it is not so, because after forty the body begins to decline while the rational faculty begins to perfect—increasing in strength. Since the rational faculty is non-corporeal, the soul is also non-corporeal; necessarily, the immateriality of the attribute entails the immateriality of the subject. Perfection: perception and intellect after forty are complete, and completeness of knowledge indicates the completeness of the rational faculty; if it were corporeal, it would weaken.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments for the immateriality of the rational soul (tajarrud al-nafs). The first argument is based on the nature of intelligible forms. If the soul were material and divisible, then the intelligible forms inhering in it would either be simple or composite. If simple, they would become divisible because they inhere in a divisible subject—a contradiction. If composite, they must resolve into simples, which would also become divisible—again a contradiction. Therefore, the soul cannot be material. The second argument observes that the rational faculty does not tire with age, unlike bodily organs. Since the body weakens after forty but the intellect strengthens, the rational faculty cannot be a bodily power; therefore the soul is immaterial. The commentary explains each step, emphasizing that the immateriality of the attribute (the rational faculty) implies the immateriality of the subject (the soul).

Key Terms
النفس
التعقل
الخلف
المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument in this passage, why cannot the soul be material?
A) Because material things are always simple.
B) Because intelligible forms would become divisible or lead to infinite regress if the soul were material.
C) Because the soul tires after forty.
D) Because the soul has position.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma of simplicity and composition in intelligible forms proves that the soul cannot be material.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar reductio argument for the immateriality of the soul using the fact that the soul can know universals, while bodily organs only perceive particulars.

The Second Proof: The Intellect's Invulnerability to Age
Second argument proving soul's immateriality from the intellect's lack of fatigue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument that the rational faculty is not bodily because it does not weaken with age.

Core Idea
What strengthens as the body weakens cannot be a bodily power.

The second proof is empirical and observational. Bodily powers tire after forty, but the intellect becomes stronger. If intellection were through a bodily organ, it would also decline. Since it does not, the rational faculty must be immaterial, and therefore the soul is immaterial.

Guided Path
1
State the claim to be proven
The claim is that intellection (ta'aqqul) is not performed through a bodily organ. If it were, it would fatigue when the body weakens.
This is the argument's starting point.
2
Observe the behavior of bodily powers
Bodily powers, like the external and internal senses, weaken after the age of forty. This is a known empirical fact.
The author uses this as a benchmark for material faculties.
3
Observe the behavior of the intellect
The intellect, on the contrary, becomes stronger and more complete after forty. Knowledge and understanding increase with age.
This contradicts what would happen if it were bodily.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the intellect does not weaken but strengthens, it cannot be a bodily power. Therefore, the rational faculty is non-corporeal, and consequently the soul is also non-corporeal (since the attribute's immateriality implies the subject's).
This is a simple modus tollens: If bodily, then weaken; not weaken; so not bodily.

Proof that Rational Souls Are Temporally Originated: A Compound Argument
A compound proof for the temporal origination of rational souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogism used to prove that rational souls originate with the body and assess each sub-argument's validity.

Core Idea
Rational souls cannot pre-exist because any supposed difference before bodies is impossible by essence, concomitants, or accidents.

This passage presents a key argument from the Peripatetic tradition regarding the origin of the rational soul. By breaking down the argument into its logical components, you will see how multiple syllogisms combine to refute the pre-existence claim. This analysis will sharpen your skill in handling compound arguments in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The passage concludes that rational souls are originated with the body (حادثة الأبدان). What is the main claim being proven?
2
Extract the overall conditional
The author argues: if souls existed before bodies, then they would have to differ in one of three ways (essence, concomitants, accidents). This is a conditional with a three-part consequent.
3
Examine each sub-argument
For each mode of difference, find the reasoning that shows it is impossible. How does the author refute difference by essence? By concomitants? By accidents?
4
Check the logical structure
Notice how the refutation of each alternative leads to the denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). The argument is compound because it chains multiple inferences.
5
Evaluate the strength
Consider whether the argument successfully eliminates all possibilities. Is there any other mode of difference unaddressed?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفوس الناطقة: اختلف الحكماء في أن النفوس الناطقة هل هي حادثة الأبدان أم موجودة من قبل؟ فذهب المشاؤون إلى أنها حادثة الأبدان، واختار المصنف وحیه مسلكهم، واستدل عليه بأن النفوس لو كانت موجودة قبل البدن فالاختلاف بينها إما أن يكون بالماهية أو بلوازمها أو بعوارضها المفارقة، والكل باطل، فوجودها قبل البدن باطل، فيكون مع البدن أما بطلان الاختلاف بالماهية فلأن ماهية الكل واحدة؛ لشمول الحد الواحد للكل، ولو كانت مختلفة لامتنع صدق الحد الواحد عليها، وأما بطلان الاختلاف باللوازم فلما مر من اتحاد الماهية؛ لأن اتحاد الملزوم يستلزم اتحاد اللازم، وأما بطلان الاختلاف بالعوارض المفارقة فلأن اختلاف العوارض إنما يكون باختلاف استعداد المادة؛ لأن تلك العوارض لا تقتضي الماهية لذاتها، وإلا لزم أن تكون تلك العوارض لازمة لها، وهذا خلف، فلا بد هناك من قابل العوارض مختلفة باستعداد مختلف وما هو إلا البدن، وإذا فرض هناك وجودها قبل البدن لم يكن اختلافها بالعوارض المفارقة الاحقة من جهة البدن، فلما أنحاء الاختلاف قبل البدن، وكان الاختلاف لازما لها؛ ضرورة اختلاف بطل جميع التشخصات، بطل وجودها قبل البدن.
The rational souls: the philosophers differed regarding whether the rational souls are originated with the bodies or existed before. The Peripatetics held that they are originated with the bodies, and the author chose their path and argued for it: if the souls existed before the body, then their differences would be either by essence and its concomitants or by accidental attributes—but all are invalid. Hence their existence before the body is invalid; thus they are with the body. As for the invalidity of difference by essence, because the essence of all is one, due to the single definition covering all; if they differed, it would be impossible for one definition to apply to them all. As for the invalidity of difference by concomitants, due to the unity of essence previously mentioned, because unity of the subject entails unity of the concomitant. As for the invalidity of difference by accidental attributes, because difference in accidents only arises from difference in the preparedness of matter; since those accidents are not necessitated by the essence itself—otherwise they would be necessary concomitants, a contradiction—so there must be a recipient of accidents with varying preparedness, and that is only the body. If their existence before the body is assumed, there would be no difference by accidental attributes consequent from the body. Since all modes of difference before the body are invalidated, and difference is necessary for them (necessarily different individuations), their existence before the body is invalidated.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that rational souls are temporally originated (haditha) with the body, not pre-existent. The author summarizes the Peripatetic position and provides a proof: if souls existed before bodies, they would have to differ in some way—either by their essence, by essential concomitants, or by accidental attributes. Difference by essence is impossible because all rational souls share the same definition (hadd), so their essence is one. Difference by concomitants is also impossible because a single essence has a single set of necessary concomitants. The only possible difference is by accidental attributes, but accidents require a material substrate with varying dispositions, which is the body. Therefore, without bodies, souls could not differ, yet we observe that souls are distinct. Hence they cannot pre-exist; they originate with the body.

Key Terms
النفوس الناطقة (al-nufus al-natiqa): Rational souls, the intellective aspect of the human soul, the subject of the debate on pre-existence. [Note: In some texts, nafs natiqa is the highest faculty; here it is the soul itself as a substance.]
حادثة (haditha): Temporally originated; coming into existence at a specific time, specifically with the body. [Note: In kalam, hadith can mean created ex nihilo; here it means with the body, not necessarily from nothing.]
الماهية (al-mahiyya): Essence; whatness; the quiddity that defines a thing; here the shared essence of all rational souls. [Note: Stable term in falsafa and mantiq.]
العوارض المفارقة (al-'awarid al-mufariqa): Accidental attributes that are separable from the essence, not necessary; they require an external cause for variation. [Note: In logic, 'arad mufariq contrasts with 'arad lazim; here it specifically refers to accidents that depend on the body.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why can't rational souls differ by essence?
A) Because essences are always different.
B) Because they share a single definition, so their essence is one.
C) Because accidents prevent essential difference.
D) Because the body causes essential difference.

Key Takeaway
Compound arguments in Islamic philosophy often combine disjunctive syllogisms with conditional proofs to establish a conclusion by eliminating alternatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the argument in a formal syllogistic format, labeling each premise as P1, P2, etc., and indicate which rule (e.g., modus tollens, disjunctive syllogism) is used in each step.
?
Identify a real theological debate where a similar compound argument is used (e.g., the proof for the world's creation in kalam) and compare its structure.

Proof and Critique: Can a Simple Cause Produce Two Effects?
Author argues that a cause of two effects is composite, then records an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that a cause of two effects is composite and evaluate the objection that the two aspects are merely mental considerations.

Core Idea
A cause yielding two effects forces composition – unless the 'aspects' are mental, not real.

This lesson presents a classic argument in Islamic metaphysics: if a single cause produces two distinct effects, then the cause must have multiple aspects, hence composition. However, a powerful objection emerges: the two aspects are mere mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) with no extramental existence, so they do not imply real composition. We will walk through the logical steps and the objection to understand the debate about simplicity and composition in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
Locate the claim 'what produces two effects is composite' in the Arabic. This is the conclusion the author aims to prove.
The thesis is stated at the beginning: 'لأن ما يصدر عنه أثران فهو مركب'.
2
Extract the reasoning chain
The argument proceeds: the aspect of the cause producing effect A is different from the aspect producing effect B. If these aspects are part of the essence, composition follows. If they are external, the cause is their source, leading to infinite regress or eventual composition.
Follow the structure: 'فمجموع هذين المفهومين أو أحدهما إن كان داخلا... وإن كانا خارجين...'
3
Formalize as a categorical syllogism
Construct a syllogism: Major premise: Everything that produces two effects has distinct aspects (and thus is composite). Minor premise: This cause produces two effects. Conclusion: Therefore, this cause is composite.
The middle term is 'producing two effects'. The argument implicitly uses the principle that distinct effects require distinct aspects.
4
Identify the objection
Find the objection that the two concepts are mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) with no external reality. They exist only in the intellect when relating cause and effect.
The objection is introduced by 'قيل عليه إن هذين المفهومين من الاعتبارات العقلية'.
5
Assess the objection's force
If the aspects are mental, they do not require real composition in the cause. The argument's assumption that the aspects are real is undermined, leaving the thesis unresolved.
The objection does not refute the logical structure but challenges the ontological status of the premises.

فصل في العلة والمعلول يصدر عنها أكثر من الواحد؛ لأن ما يصدر عنه أثران فهو مركب؛ لأن كون الشيء بحيث يصدر عنه هذا الأثر غير كونه بحيث يصدر عنه ذلك الأثر، فمجموع هذين المفهومين أو أحدهما إن كان داخلا في ذات المصدر لزم التركيب في ذاته، وإن كانا خارجين كان مصدرا لهما، فكونه مصدرا لهذا غير كونه مصدرا لذلك، فينتهي لا محالة إلى ما يوجب التركيب والكثرة في الذات ونقول أيضا إن المعلول يجب وجوده عند وجود علته التامة أعني إن ما يصدر عنه أثران فهو مركب بأن كون الشيء بحيث يصدر عنه هذا الأثر مغاير لكونه بحيث يصدر عنه ذلك الأثر؛ لإمكان تعقل كل واحد منهما بدون الآخر، وإذا ثبت في الفاعل الذي صدر عنه أثران مفهومان متغايران، فمجموع هذين المفهومين أو أحدهما إن كان داخلا في ذاته لزم التركيب في ذاته، وإن كانا خارجين كان ذلك الفاعل مصدرا لهما؛ إذ لو كانا مستندين إلى غيره لما كان ذلك الفاعل مصدرا للأثرين؛ لكون الأثرين مستندين إلى ذينك المفهومين، والمقدر خلافه، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى المفهومين الخارجين، فإما أن تذهب السلسلة إلى غير النهاية أو تنتهي جهة الكثرة في الذات فيلزم التركيب، فثبت أن ما يصدر عنه أثران فهو مركب. وإن كانا إلخ قيل عليه إن هذين المفهومين من الاعتبارات العقلية، ليس لهما تحقق في الخارج حتى يطلب لهما علة ومصدر يحتاج إلى اعتبار الكثرة في مصدرهما حتى ينتهى إلى التركيب والكثرة في الذات، قال السيد فإن العقل إذا لاحظ الشيء مقيسا إلى معلوله أدرك لهما إضافتين، أعني المصدرية والصادرية، فهما أمران إضافيان عارضان لهما في العقل متأخران عنهما في التعقل ولا وجود لهما في الخارج أصلا؛ إذ ليس في الخارج إلا ذات المصدر أعني العلة وذات الصادر أعني المعلول، وليس كون الأول مصدرا وكون الثاني صادرا من الأمور المحققة في الأعيان.
Section on cause and effect: it produces more than one; because what produces two effects is composite; because the thing being such that it produces this effect is other than its being such that it produces that effect. So the sum of these two concepts or one of them, if it is included in the essence of the source, composition in its essence follows; and if they are external, it is a source for them. So its being a source for this is other than its being a source for that, thus it inevitably ends in what necessitates composition and multiplicity in the essence. We also say that the effect must exist when its complete cause exists, meaning: what produces two effects is composite, because the thing being such that it produces this effect is different from its being such that it produces that effect, since each can be conceived without the other. And if in the agent that produced two effects there are established two distinct concepts, then the sum of these two concepts or one of them, if it is included in its essence, composition in its essence follows; and if they are external, then that agent is a source for them; for if they were attributed to another, that agent would not be the source of the two effects, because the two effects are attributed to those two concepts, and the presupposed is contrary to that. Then we transfer the discussion to the two external concepts: either the chain goes to infinity, or it ends in the aspect of multiplicity in the essence, so composition follows. Thus it is established that what produces two effects is composite. - And if they are external etc. It was objected to it: these two concepts are mental considerations; they have no reality in the external so that a cause and source be sought for them, requiring consideration of multiplicity in their source until it ends in composition and multiplicity in the essence. Al-Sayyid said: For when the intellect observes a thing in relation to its effect, it apprehends for them two relations, namely source-ship and effect-ship; they are two relational matters that occur to them in the intellect, posterior to them in intellection, and have no existence in the external at all; for in the external there is only the essence of the source, i.e., the cause, and the essence of the effect, i.e., the effect; and the first being a source and the second being an effect are not among things realized in external entities.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument proving that any cause producing two distinct effects must be composite (i.e., have multiple aspects or parts). The author begins by stating the principle: 'what produces two effects is composite.' The proof proceeds by noting that the aspect of the cause that produces the first effect is different from the aspect that produces the second effect, since each can be conceived separately. If these two aspects are part of the cause's essence, then the cause is composite. If they are external to the essence, then the cause is the source of those aspects, leading to an infinite regress or eventual composition. Thus, anyone who asserts that a single cause (like a simple divine agent) directly produces multiple effects must admit composition in that cause. The passage then records an objection by 'al-Sayyid' (likely a commentator): the two 'aspects' are merely mental considerations (i'tibarāt 'aqliyya) with no extramental reality; they are relational attributes that arise when the intellect compares the cause to its effect. Therefore, they do not require a real cause and do not imply real composition. The objection is presented without an explicit answer in this lesson, leaving the argument open to further discussion.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla): The cause that produces an effect; in this context, the source (maṣdar) from which the effect issues. [Note: No significant shift.]
معلول (maʿlūl): The effect that is produced by the cause. [Note: No significant shift.]
أثر (athar): The effect or consequence of a cause; used interchangeably with maʿlūl. [Note: No significant shift.]
مفهوم (mafhūm): A concept or mental notion; the two concepts are the 'aspects' of source-ship and effect-ship. [Note: In later logic, mafhūm can also mean 'meaning' or 'intension'.]
اعتبار عقلي (iʿtibār ʿaqlī): A mental consideration; something that exists only in the mind, not in external reality. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if a single cause produces two effects, what must be true of that cause?
A) It must be simple.
B) It must be composite.
C) It must be eternal.
D) It must be contingent.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that a cause of multiple effects must be composite unless the distinguishing aspects are merely mental considerations, highlighting the importance of ontology in logical proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the two 'concepts' (مفهومان) in the argument and explain whether the objection considers them real or mental. Then evaluate which side of the debate you find more convincing based on the logical tools.
?
Construct a similar argument to prove that if a simple cause produces three distinct effects, it must be composite. Then present the objection that the three aspects are mental considerations. Which principle is being contested?

The Effect is Necessary Under the Complete Cause
Author proves the necessity of the effect under the complete cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism proving that the effect is necessary (wājib) when its complete cause is present, and distinguish essential necessity from necessity through another.

Core Idea
The complete cause necessitates its effect, but the effect remains possible in itself.

In Islamic philosophy, the relation between cause and effect is understood through the concept of 'complete cause' (al-ʿilla al-tāmma). When all conditions for an effect are fully realized, the effect cannot fail to exist. The author uses a disjunctive argument to prove this, and then clarifies that this necessity is derived from the cause, not intrinsic to the effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proven
The claim is that the effect is wājib al-wujūd (necessary in existence) when the complete cause is present. Look for the phrase 'يجب وجوده عند تحقق العلة التامة'.
2
Set up the disjunction
The author considers three possibilities at the moment the complete cause is present: the effect is either impossible (mumtaniʿ), possible (mumkin), or necessary (wājib). Recognize that these three are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
The disjunction is implicit in the text: 'فإما أن يكون ممتنع الوجود أو ممكن الوجود' but necessary is the remaining option.
3
Rule out the impossible
If the effect were impossible at that moment, it could never exist. But it does exist (since the cause is complete). Therefore, impossibility is absurd.
The text says: 'وهو محال وإلا لما وجد'.
4
Rule out the possible
If the effect were possible, it would still need a preponderant (murajjiḥ) to bring it into actuality. But the complete cause already includes all necessary conditions. Thus, possibility would contradict the completeness of the cause.
The text argues: 'فيحتاج إلى مرجح... فلا يكون جملة الأمور المعتبرة في وجوده حاصلة'.
5
Conclude necessity through another
Since the effect cannot be impossible or possible, it must be necessary. However, this necessity is not intrinsic; it is derived from the cause. In itself, the effect is possible (mumkin bi'l-dhāt).
The text concludes: 'فيكون واجبا لغيره ممكنا بالذات'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند تحقق جملة الأمور المعتبرة في تحققه؛ لأنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجود حينئذ، فإما أن يكون ممتنع الوجود، وهو محال وإلا لما وجد، أو ممكن الوجود، فيحتاج إلى مرجح يخرجه من القوة إلى الفعل، فلا يكون جملة الأمور المعتبرة في وجوده حاصلة، وقد فرضناها حاصلة، هذا خلف فبان أن المعلول يجب وجوده عند تحقق العلة التامة، فيكون واجبا لغيره ممكنا بالذات؛ لأنا لو اعتبرنا ماهيته من حيث هي هي لا يجب لها الوجود ولا العدم. هداية: كون الشيء موجودا لا ينافي تأثير العلة الفاعلية فيه، لأن الشيء عند تحقق إلخ قيل عليه إن هذا التفسير للعلة التامة لا يصدق إذا كانت بسيطة كالباري للعقل الأول عند الفلاسفة، وأجيب عنه أنه لا بد من اعتبار إمكان المعلول حتى يصح تأثير الفاعل، فالتركيب لازم. ممكن الوجود أي أن يكون وجود المعلول وعدمه متساويين لا ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخر حين وجود العلة التامة، فلا تكفي العلة التامة لترجيح الوجود على العدم، فاحتيج إلى مرجح آخر، فصار وجود المعلول موقوفا على مرجح آخر، فلا تكون جملة الأمور المعتبرة في وجوده متحققة فلاح أن ما فرضناه من قبل أنه علة تامة لم تكن علة تامة، وهذا خلف، فلما تبين أن وجود المعلول حين وجود العلة التامة ليس بممتنع ولا بممكن، أنه واجب؛ لانحصار أحوال المفهومات في ثلاثة وهذا الوجوب ليس ذاتيا بل غيريا، وإذا اعتبر المعلول من حيث هو هو مع قطع النظر عن علته فليس بضروري الوجود والعدم؛ لأن الواجب بالذات والممتنع بالذات كلاهما مستغنيان عن فيكون ممكن الوجود؛ لأن الإمكان عبارة عن عدم ضرورة الوجود والعدم. هداية: أوردها لدفع اشتباه ووهم يرد ههنا، وهو أن تأثير العلة الفاعلية إما حين العدم فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين؛ لأن معنى التأثير هو إفادة الوجود، فلما كان إفادة تعين العلة، .
When the totality of the things considered in its realization is realized; because if it were not necessary in existence at that time, then it would either be impossible in existence, which is absurd because then it would not exist, or possible in existence, in which case it needs a preponderant to bring it from potentiality to actuality, so the totality of the things considered in its existence would not be realized, yet we have supposed it realized; this is a contradiction. So it becomes clear that the effect must exist when the complete cause is realized, so it is necessary through another, possible in itself; because if we consider its quiddity in itself, existence and non-existence are not necessary for it. Guidance: A thing's being existent does not negate the influence of the efficient cause in it, because the thing when realized etc. It was objected to it: this interpretation of the complete cause is not true if it is simple, like the Creator (al-Bāri') for the first intellect according to the philosophers. And it was answered: it is necessary to consider the possibility of the effect for the agent's influence to be valid, so composition is necessary. Possible existence means that the existence and non-existence of the effect are equal, with no preponderance of one over the other when the complete cause exists. So the complete cause would not suffice to preponderate existence over non-existence, thus another preponderant is needed, so the existence of the effect becomes dependent on another preponderant, so the totality of the things considered in its existence would not be realized. So it becomes clear that what we previously supposed to be the complete cause was not the complete cause; this is a contradiction. So when it is shown that the existence of the effect when the complete cause exists is neither impossible nor possible, that it is necessary; because the states of concepts are limited to three, and this necessity is not essential but accidental. And if the effect is considered in itself, apart from its cause, it is not necessary in existence or non-existence; because the necessary by itself and the impossible by itself are both independent of, so it is possible in existence; because possibility is the absence of necessity of existence and non-existence. Guidance: It was brought to remove a confusion and illusion that occurs here, namely that the influence of the efficient cause is either at the time of non-existence, which leads to the conjunction of two contradictories, because the meaning of influence is bestowing existence. So when it is bestowing, the determination of the cause...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the effect is necessary (wājib) when its complete cause (tāmma) is present, a standard doctrine in Islamic philosophy. The argument proceeds by disjunctive syllogism: if the effect were not necessary at the moment of the complete cause, then it would be either impossible or possible. Impossible is absurd because then it could not exist at all. Possible would require an additional preponderant (murajjiḥ) to bring it into existence, contradicting the assumption that the complete cause is already fully realized. Hence, the effect must be necessary, but this necessity is 'through another' (bi'l-ghayr) — it is 'necessary by another' (wājib bi'l-ghayr), while in itself it is possible (mumkin bi'l-dhāt). The text then addresses an objection: the definition of 'complete cause' fails for a simple cause like the First Cause (al-Bāri') producing the first intellect, because the First Cause is simple yet produces an effect. The answer is that even here, the possibility (imkān) of the effect must be considered, implying a 'composition' (tarkīb) in the cause-effect relation. The text concludes by clarifying that the effect's necessity is not essential but relational, and that possibility (imkān) means the absence of necessity of existence or non-existence. The final 'Guidance' (hidāya) warns against a confusion: if the efficient cause's influence occurs when the effect is non-existent, then existence and non-existence would coexist; but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
علة تامة
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if we consider the effect in itself (apart from its cause), what is its status?
A) Necessary existence
B) Impossible existence
C) Possible existence
D) Non-existent

Key Takeaway
The complete cause necessitates the effect, but the effect remains contingent in its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a disjunctive syllogism to show that if the conditions for a valid legal contract (consent, offer, acceptance) are completely fulfilled, the contract is valid and binding. Use the three states: impossible, possible, necessary.
?
Identify the three states (impossible, possible, necessary) in the argument that the Prophet's message is true: if it were false, it would be impossible for miracles to support it; if it were possible, it would need additional confirmation; therefore it is necessary.

Addressing the Objection to the Complete Cause Definition for a Simple Cause
Author responds to an objection regarding the definition of complete cause for a simple cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the objection that the definition of complete cause fails for a simple cause like al-Bāri', and explain why composition (tarkīb) is necessary in the causal relation.

Core Idea
Even a simple cause like the Creator requires composition when considering the effect's possibility.

After proving that the effect is necessary when the complete cause is present, the author anticipates an objection: the definition seems to fail for a simple cause, such as the First Cause (al-Bāri') creating the first intellect. The answer shows that possibility (imkān) of the effect introduces a kind of composition in the causal relation, preserving the principle.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The opponent claims that the definition of 'complete cause' does not apply when the cause is simple, like al-Bāri' in relation to the first intellect. A simple cause has no multiplicity, yet the definition implies multiple conditions.
The text says: 'لا يصدق إذا كانت بسيطة كالباري للعقل الأول'.
2
Identify the answer
The answer is that the effect's possibility (imkān) must be considered. This introduces a relational composition (tarkīb) not in the cause itself but in the causality relation.
The answer: 'لا بد من اعتبار إمكان المعلول حتى يصح تأثير الفاعل، فالتركيب لازم'.
3
Relate to the earlier proof
The possibility of the effect is what requires a preponderant in the disjunctive syllogism. In the case of a simple cause, this possibility is still present, and it is accounted for in the complete cause's completeness.
4
Recognise the theological implication
This response preserves God's simplicity while maintaining that His creative act is conditioned by the possibility of the creature. Composition is only in the causal relation, not in God's essence.

Source Text

The Trilemma of Efficient Causality and Its Resolution
Author resolves a trilemma about the timing of causal influence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a disjunctive trilemma in a causal argument and identify the fallacy of tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
The efficient cause gives existence at the moment of existence, not before—otherwise contradictions arise.

This lesson tackles a classic objection in Islamic philosophy: if the cause gives existence to the effect, does it do so when the effect is non-existent or when it exists? The objector presents a trilemma, but the author shows that only one option is coherent, and the apparent fallacy of 'obtaining the obtained' is avoided by a careful distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trilemma
Find the three possibilities for when the efficient cause brings about existence: at the time of non-existence, at the time of existence, or at both times.
The text explicitly states these three options.
2
Eliminate contradictory options
The first option (non-existence) and third option (both) lead to the conjunction of existence and non-existence, which is contradictory. So they are invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the remaining option and the objection
Only the second option remains: influence at the time of existence. But the objector argues this implies tahsil al-hasil (obtaining the obtained).
Tahsil al-hasil is considered a logical fallacy.
4
Understand the author's answer
The author replies that the existence at that time is not pre-existing; it is precisely the product of the influence. The distinction is between 'influence at the time of existence' and 'influence conditional upon existence.'
The cause does not require the effect's prior existence as a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هداية: أوردها لدفع اشتباه ووهم يرد ههنا، وهو أن تأثير العلة الفاعلية إما حين العدم فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين؛ لأن معنى التأثير هو إفادة الوجود، فلما كان إفادة إذا كان معدوما ثم يوجد، فإما أن توصف العلة بكونها مفيدة لوجوده حالة العدم أو حالة الوجود أو في الحالتين جميعا، لا جائز أن تفيد وجوده حالة العدم أو في الحالتين، وإلا لزم اجتماع الوجود والعدم، هذا خلف، فإذن تفيد وجوده حالة وجوده المفاد، فكون الشيء موجودا لا ينافي كونه معلولا. الوجود في زمان العدم لزم اجتماع الوجود والعدم في زمان واحد، وهو اجتماع النقيضين أو حين الوجود، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل فأجاب عنه باختيار الشق الثاني، ولا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل؛ لأن الوجود في زمانه مفاد من تأثيره لا أنه شرط لتأثيره حتى يكون حاصلا قبل التأثير، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل، وبالجملة: فرق بين التأثير في زمان الوجود وبين التأثير بشرط الوجود. له أن ثم يوجد إلخ "ثم" ههنا لمجرد التراخي لا التراخي في الزمان؛ لأن المعلول القديم ليس عدم زماني وإلا لم يبق قديما ومعنى كون عدم الممكن سابقا على وجوده الممكن إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو لا يجب له الوجود بل هو معرى عن معنى الوجود، ثم إذا اعتبر صدوره عن الجاعل ووصف بالوجود، فإفادة الجاعل وجوده في أية حالة هي؟ فأفاد المصنف له أن الجاعل أفاد الوجود حالة الوجود، وأبطل إفادته الوجود حالة العدم أو في الحالتين؛ للزوم اجتماع النقيضين، وكثير من الناس يظنون أن تأثير الفاعل حالة العدم ووجود المفعول بعد ذلك، ومنشأ هذا التوهم أن مفعولاتهم مترتبة على تأثيراتهم بعد زمان التأثير، وهذا التوهم فاسد؛ لأن حركاتهم ليست جاعلة لشيء إنما هي معدات والمعد ما يقتضي وجود الشيء بعد عدمه، والذي يظهر فيه تأثير قدرتهم بحسب الظاهر هو حركاتهم، وليس تأثير القدرة منفكا عن وجود حركاتهم، فتأمل حق التأمل حتى لا يضرك الوهم الباطل.
Guidance: He presented it to remove confusion and a false notion that occurs here, namely that the influence of the efficient cause is either at the time of non-existence, which necessitates the conjunction of contradictories, because the meaning of influence is to bring about existence. Since it is bringing about [existence] when it was non-existent then it exists, so either the cause is described as bringing about its existence in the state of non-existence or in the state of existence or in both states together. It is not permissible that it brings about its existence in the state of non-existence or in both, otherwise the conjunction of existence and non-existence would occur; this is contradictory. Thus it brings about its existence in the state of its brought-about existence. So that a thing exists does not negate that it is an effect. [The objection:] Existence at the time of non-existence would necessitate the conjunction of existence and non-existence in one time – that is the conjunction of contradictories – or at the time of existence, necessitating obtaining what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil). He answered by choosing the second alternative, and obtaining what is already obtained does not follow, because the existence at its time is produced from its influence, not that it is a condition for its influence such that it would be already present before the influence, thereby necessitating obtaining what is already obtained. In summary: There is a difference between influence in the time of existence and influence conditional upon existence. He has that 'then exists' etc. 'Then' here is for mere succession, not temporal succession, because the eternal effect is not temporally non-existent, otherwise it would not remain eternal. The meaning of the non-existence of the possible being prior to its existence is that when considered in itself, existence is not necessary for it; rather it is devoid of the meaning of existence. Then when its emanation from the Maker is considered and it is described with existence, what state is the Maker's bringing about of its existence? So the author informed him that the Maker brings about existence in the state of existence, and invalidated bringing about existence in the state of non-existence or in both, because of the necessity of conjunction of contradictories. Many people think that the influence of the agent is in the state of non-existence and the existence of the effect occurs afterward. The origin of this misconception is that their effects are ordered after their influences after a time of influence. This misconception is false, because their movements do not make something; they are only preparations (mu'iddat). The preparer is what necessitates the existence of a thing after its non-existence. What appears to be the effect of their power externally is their movements, and the influence of power is not separated from the existence of their movements. So reflect deeply so that false imagination does not harm you.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle objection regarding the temporal relationship between cause and effect. The objection presents a trilemma: the efficient cause's influence either occurs (1) when the effect is non-existent, (2) when it exists, or (3) both. Option (1) leads to the contradiction of existence and non-existence coexisting. Option (3) similarly leads to contradiction. So only option (2) remains. However, option (2) seems to lead to 'tahsil al-hasil' – obtaining what is already obtained – because if the effect already exists, what need is there for the cause to bring it into existence? The author's answer clarifies that there is a distinction between 'influence at the time of existence' and 'influence conditional upon existence.' The cause does not require the effect's prior existence as a condition; rather, the effect's existence is precisely the product of the influence at that moment. Thus no tahsil al-hasil occurs. The passage then explains that for eternal effects (like the First Intellect), the term 'then' (thumma) indicates logical priority, not temporal priority. The common human experience of a time gap between an agent's action and its effect is due to the fact that human actions are merely preparatory (mu'iddat), not true creation. For example, a person's movement to write is a preparation for the writing, not the cause of its existence in a true sense. The real efficient cause (like the Divine) brings about existence directly without temporal delay. This refutes the false imagination that causality necessarily implies a temporal gap.

Key Terms
التأثير
تحصيل الحاصل
العلة الفاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical fallacy of 'tahsil al-hasil' as discussed in this passage?
A) Assuming the cause must precede the effect in time.
B) Demanding that something already existing be brought into existence.
C) Assuming the cause and effect are simultaneous.
D) Denying efficient causality altogether.

Key Takeaway
The efficient cause gives existence at the moment of the effect's existence, and this is not a case of tahsil al-hasil because the existence is not prior to the causal influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a disjunctive trilemma about whether the soul acquires knowledge before or during the act of learning, then eliminate contradictory options.
?
Identify the fallacy of tahsil al-hasil in the following argument: 'The ruling of prayer is already established in the Quran, so there is no need for evidence from Hadith to prove it.' Explain why it is fallacious.

True Causation vs. Mere Preparation (I'dad)
Author distinguishes true causation from preparation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a true efficient cause (fa'il) and a preparer (mu'idd) and explain why human actions are only preparatory.

Core Idea
Human actions prepare the effect; only the Divine cause truly creates existence directly.

The passage ends by noting that many mistakenly think causality requires a temporal gap because they observe human actions. But human actions are mere preparations (mu'iddat), not true creation. This lesson clarifies the distinction between i'dad and ta'thir, which is crucial for understanding divine causality in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mu'idd
A preparer (mu'idd) is something that necessitates the existence of a thing after its non-existence, but it does not give existence directly.
The text says: 'المعد ما يقتضي وجود الشيء بعد عدمه'.
2
Contrast with true cause
A true efficient cause gives existence at the moment of its influence, without any temporal gap. The effect is simultaneous with the cause's action.
The author emphasizes the difference between 'influence at the time of existence' and 'influence conditional upon existence'.
3
Apply to human actions
Human movements, like writing, are not true causes of the written words; they are preparations. The real cause is God, who creates the writing at the moment of the movement.
The text says: 'حركاتهم ليست جاعلة لشيء إنما هي معدات'.

Source Text

The Cause Gives Existence Only at the Time of the Effect
Author proves the cause gives existence only at the time of the effect's existence, not before.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's disjunctive proof that the cause gives existence at the time of the effect's existence, and distinguish between influencing at the time of existence and influencing subject to the condition of existence.

Core Idea
The cause creates existence exactly when the effect exists, not before – otherwise contradiction or redundancy.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a rigorous proof about the timing of causal influence. The author uses a disjunctive syllogism to eliminate impossible options and then addresses a subtle objection about redundancy. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping the relationship between cause and effect in Islamic philosophy and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
The author lists three options for when the cause gives existence to an effect that was nonexistent: at the time of nonexistence, at the time of existence, or at both times. Write them down as three distinct disjuncts.
The disjunction is assumed exhaustive for the argument.
2
Examine the elimination of the first and third options
The author argues that the first option (at nonexistence) and the third (both) are impossible because they would require existence and nonexistence to coexist at the same time, which is a logical contradiction (اجتماع النقيضين).
This is a proof by contradiction: if the cause gave existence before the effect existed, then the effect would both exist (from the cause) and not exist (since it's still nonexistent) simultaneously.
3
Accept the remaining option
With the first and third eliminated, the only remaining possibility is that the cause gives existence at the time of the effect's existence. The author states: 'Therefore, it gives its existence at the time of its given existence.'
This conclusion is forced by the disjunctive syllogism.
4
Analyze the objection of tahsil al-hasil
An objector might say: if the effect already exists at the time the cause gives existence, then the cause is obtaining what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل), which is redundant and absurd.
This objection threatens the validity of the conclusion.
5
Understand the author's response
The author replies that the existence at its time is given by the cause's effect (act), not a condition for that effect. The cause does not require prior existence to act. There is a crucial difference between 'influencing at the time of existence' and 'influencing subject to the condition of existence'.
In other words, the effect's existence does not precede the cause's action; it is simultaneous with it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كان معدوما ثم يوجد، فإما أن توصف العلة بكونها مفيدة لوجوده حالة العدم أو حالة الوجود أو في الحالتين جميعا، لا جائز أن تفيد وجوده حالة العدم أو في الحالتين، وإلا لزم اجتماع الوجود والعدم، هذا خلف، فإذن تفيد وجوده حالة وجوده المفاد، فكون الشيء موجودا لا ينا في كونه معلولا. الوجود في زمان العدم لزم اجتماع الوجود والعدم في زمان واحد، وهو اجتماع النقيضين أو حين الوجود، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل فأجاب عنه باختيار الشق الثاني، ولا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل؛ لأن الوجود في زمانه مفاد من تأثيره لا أنه شرط لتأثيره حتى يكون حاصلا قبل التأثير، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل، وبالجملة: فرق بين التأثير في زمان الوجود وبين التأثير بشرط الوجود.
If something is nonexistent then exists, the cause is described as giving its existence either at the time of nonexistence or at the time of existence or in both states. It is not possible that it gives its existence at the time of nonexistence or in both states, otherwise the coexistence of existence and nonexistence would follow, which is absurd. Therefore, it gives its existence at the time of its given existence. So the fact that something exists does not prevent it from being an effect. If existence were at the time of nonexistence, the coexistence of existence and nonexistence would follow in one time, which is the conjunction of contradictories; or if at the time of existence, then obtaining what is already obtained would follow. He answered by choosing the second option, and obtaining what is already obtained does not follow because the existence at its time is given by his effect, not that it is a condition for his effect such that it would be obtained before the effect, in which case obtaining what is already obtained would follow. In summary: there is a difference between influencing at the time of existence and influencing subject to the condition of existence.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof about the timing of causal influence. The author considers three possibilities for when the cause gives existence to an effect that was nonexistent: at the time of nonexistence, at the time of existence, or both. The first and third are refuted because they would entail the coexistence of existence and nonexistence simultaneously, which is a contradiction. Therefore, the cause must give existence only at the time the effect exists. A potential objection is raised: if the cause gives existence at the time of existence, that would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل) because the effect already exists at that time. The answer distinguishes: the existence at its time is produced by the cause's effect, not a precondition. The cause does not require existence prior to its action. Thus, there is a crucial difference between influencing at the time of existence and influencing conditional on prior existence.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the cause not give existence at the time of the effect's nonexistence?
A) Because it would lead to a contradiction of existence and nonexistence
B) Because the cause cannot act on what does not exist
C) Because the effect must be present for an action
D) Because it would require multiple causes

Key Takeaway
The cause gives existence to the effect at the very moment the effect exists, and this is not redundant because the effect's existence is precisely what the cause brings about.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a disjunctive argument on the following: A ruling (hukm) must be derived either from the Quran, the Sunnah, or personal opinion (ra'y). The third option is invalid according to scholarly consensus. Use the same elimination method to conclude that a valid ruling must be from Quran or Sunnah. Explain each step.
?
Analyze the objection of tahsil al-hasil in a different domain: If a mujtahid says 'the ruling exists at the time of my ijtihad', does that make the ijtihad redundant? Apply the author's distinction between influencing at the time of existence and influencing subject to the condition of existence.

Proof that the Necessary Being's Existence is Not Acquired
Author disproves that existence is acquired in the Necessary Being

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism used to prove that the Necessary Being's existence is self-sufficient and not acquired.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence cannot depend on anything—not even itself.

In this passage, the author uses a powerful disjunctive proof to show that the existence of the Necessary Being is not something acquired from another. He assumes the opposite and then demonstrates that it leads to an absurdity in every possible case. This argument is a cornerstone of Islamic Kalam for establishing God's self-sufficiency. By mastering this reasoning, you will learn how to use a disjunctive syllogism to prove a metaphysical truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption being tested
The author assumes that existence is dependent on another (i.e., acquired). This is the initial hypothesis to be disproven.
This is a proof by contradiction, so we assume the opposite of what we want to prove.
2
Derive the consequence of the assumption
If existence is dependent, then the thing (the Necessary Being) would be contingent (mumkin) and in need of an effecter (mu'aththir).
3
Set up the disjunction about the effecter
The effecter must be either the same essence or something else. These are the only two possibilities, forming a complete disjunction.
This is a disjunctive syllogism: either A or B, both lead to absurdity, so the original assumption is false.
4
Examine the first option: effecter is the essence itself
If the effecter is the essence, then the cause must exist before the effect. But the effect is the existence of the essence, so the essence would have to exist before itself—a contradiction.
5
Examine the second option: effecter is something else
If the effecter is other than the essence, then the Necessary Being would depend on another for its existence, which contradicts its necessity. This is also impossible.

Source Text

فصل في أن وجوب الوجود. لها كان الوجود من حيث هو مفتقرا إلى الغير، فيكون ممكنا لذاته، فلا بد له من مؤثر، وذلك المؤثر إن كان نفس تلك الحقيقة يلزم أن تكون موجودة قبل الوجود؛ لأن العلة الموجدة للشيء يجب تقدمها على المعلول بالوجود، فيكون الشيء موجودا قبل نفسه، هذا خلف، وإن كان غير تلك الماهية يلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته محتاجا إلى الغير في الوجود، وهذا محال.
Section on that the necessity of existence. If existence, inasmuch as it is dependent on another, then it is contingent in itself, so it must have an effecter. That effecter, if it is the same essence, then it would be necessary that it exists before existence, because the cause that brings something into existence must precede the effect in existence, so the thing would exist before itself—this is absurd. And if it is other than that quiddity, then the necessary in itself would be needful of another in existence—and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction for the self-sufficiency of the Necessary Being. The author begins by assuming that existence is dependent on another, which would make it contingent and in need of an effecter. The effecter is then analyzed into two possibilities. The first is that the effecter is the essence itself, which leads to a circularity: the cause would have to exist before itself. The second is that the effecter is something else, which would make the necessary dependent on another, contradicting its necessity. Both options are impossible, so the original assumption is false: existence is not dependent, and the Necessary Being's existence is not acquired from another. This argument demonstrates that the existence of the Necessary Being is not an added attribute but is identical to its essence.

A) The effecter would be unnecessary.
B) The essence would be prior to itself.
C) The effecter would be contingent.
D) The existence would be multiple.

The disjunctive syllogism shows that the Necessary Being's existence is self-sufficient, not acquired from either itself or another.

Necessity of Existence is Identical to the Essence
Proof that necessity of existence is identical to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof that the necessity of existence is not an added attribute but identical to the essence of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
If necessity were added, it would cause circularity or regress, so it must be the essence itself.

The author proves that the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) is not a separate attribute but the very essence of the Necessary Being. This proof is a cornerstone of Islamic theology and relies on a reductio ad absurdum. Follow the steps to see how the impossibility of addedness leads to the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The claim is that necessity of existence is identical to the essence (dhat) of the Necessary Being. The opposite would be that necessity is an added attribute (zā'id).
2
Assume the opposite
Assume that necessity of existence is added to the essence. Then it would be an effect (ma'lul) of the essence, since it depends on the essence.
The essence is the cause (illa) of this addition.
3
Apply the causal principle
For the cause to produce the effect, the cause itself must be necessary. Here, the essence must have its own necessity to cause the effect. This leads to a dilemma about the necessity of the cause.
4
Examine the dilemma
The necessity of the cause (essence) is either identical to the necessity of the effect (added necessity) or different. If identical, the thing precedes itself (circular). If different, an infinite regress of necessities occurs. Both are absurd.
The text states: '...either the necessity of the cause equals that of the effect, or it is different.'
5
Conclude the reductio
Since both branches of the dilemma are impossible, the original assumption (that necessity is added) is false. Therefore, necessity of existence is identical to the essence.
This is the desired conclusion (al-matlub).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن وجوب الوجود وتعينه عين ذاته. أما الأول فإن وجوب الوجود لو كان زائدا على حقيقته لكان معلولا لذاته، والعلة ما لم يجب وجودها استحال أن يوجد المعلول، وذلك به الوجوب هو الوجوب بالذات ضرورة، فيكون وجوب الوجود قبل الواجبة، ويلزم افتقار الواجب لذاته في وجوده إلى الغير فلا يكون واجبا، وهذا خلف، فلاح أن وجوده عين حقيقته وهو المطلوب. وجوب الوجود: اعلم أن وجوب الوجود وكذا تعينه قد يطلق ويراد به المفهوم المصدري وقد يراد به المنشأ، وحيث حكموا بصيغة وجوب الوجود وتعينه أرادوا المنشأ لا المفهوم المصدري والدليل على الأول: أن وجوب الوجود لو لم يكن عين حقيقة لكان عارضا لها؛ لامتناع الجزئية، وممكنا؛ لاحتياج العارض إلى المعروض، ومعلولا لذاته؛ لامتناع احتياج الواجب لذاته في وجوبه إلى الغير، والعلة ما لم يجب وجودها في نفسها لم يكن أن توجد المعلول ضرورة أن وجوب وجود المعلول متفرع على وجوب وجود العلة، فوجوب وجود العلة إن كان عين وجوب وجود المعلول يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وإن كان غيره لزم التسلسل أو ينتهي إلى وجوب هو عينه، وهو المطلوب. وأما الثاني؛ فلأن تعينه لو كان زائدا على حقيقته لكان معلولا لذاته، والعلة ما لم تكن متعينة لا توجد، فيكون التعين حاصلا قبل نفسه، وهو محال.
Section on that the necessity of existence and its determination are identical to the essence. As for the first, if the necessity of existence were additional to its reality, it would be an effect of the essence. The cause, unless its existence is necessary, cannot bring about the effect. Necessarily, the necessity of existence is the necessity by essence. So the necessity of existence would be prior to the necessary thing, leading to the necessary in itself being needful of another in its existence, so it would not be necessary—this is absurd. Thus it appears that its existence is identical to its reality, and that is what is sought. (Clarification:) Know that the necessity of existence, and similarly its determination, may be used to mean the conceptual meaning or the instantiation. Where they have judged using the expression 'necessity of existence and its determination', they meant the instantiation, not the conceptual meaning. The proof for the first: if the necessity of existence were not identical to the reality, it would be an accident of it—because it cannot be a part—and contingent, because an accident needs a subject, and an effect of the essence, because the necessary in itself cannot be in need of another for its necessity. The cause, unless its existence is necessary in itself, cannot bring about the effect, necessarily because the necessity of the effect's existence depends on the necessity of the cause's existence. So if the necessity of the cause's existence is identical to the necessity of the effect's existence, then the thing would precede itself; and if it is different, then an infinite regress would result, or it would end at a necessity that is identical to it—and that is what is sought. As for the second (determination): if its determination were additional to its reality, it would be an effect of the essence. The cause, unless it is determined, does not bring about existence. So determination would occur before itself—and this is absurd.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage contains the proof that the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) and determination (ta'ayyun) are not attributes added to the essence of the Necessary Being but are the essence itself. The author first addresses wujub al-wujud. If it were added, it would be an effect of the essence, but the cause's existence must be necessary for it to produce the effect, leading to circularity or dependence. A clarification is given about the meaning of terminology: wujub al-wujud can mean the conceptual notion or the actual instantiation (al-munsha'). The proof uses the fact that if wujub were not identical, it would be an accident, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. Then the second part proves that ta'ayyun (determination or individuation) is also identical. If added, it would be an effect, and the cause must be determined to produce an effect, leading to the determination preceding itself. Thus both are shown to be identical to the essence.

Determination is Identical to the Essence
Proof that determination is identical to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that determination (ta'ayyun) is not an added attribute but identical to the essence of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
If determination were added, it would need to preexist itself, so it must be the essence.

The second part of the passage proves that determination (ta'ayyun) is also identical to the essence. The argument is simpler but equally powerful: if added, it would be an effect, but the cause must already be determined to cause anything, leading to determination preceding itself. Follow the steps to grasp this concise proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The claim is that determination (ta'ayyun) is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being. The opposite would be that it is an added attribute.
2
Assume the opposite
Assume that determination is added to the essence. Then it would be an effect (ma'lul) of the essence.
The essence is the cause.
3
Apply the causal condition
For the cause to bring about an effect, the cause must itself be determined. The text says: 'the cause, unless it is determined, does not bring about existence.'
Determination is necessary for causation.
4
Derive the absurdity
If determination is an effect, then the cause (essence) must already be determined to produce it. This means determination would exist before itself—an obvious impossibility.
The text states: 'so determination would occur before itself—and this is absurd.'
5
Conclude the proof
Since the assumption leads to absurdity, determination cannot be added. Therefore, it is identical to the essence.
This completes the proof for the second part.

Proof of the Unity of the Necessary Being (Disjunctive Argument)
Proof of the oneness of the Necessary Being via disjunctive argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive proof for the oneness of the Necessary Being and explain why the two possible distinguishing factors lead to contradictions.

Core Idea
Two necessary beings cannot exist because their distinction would contradict the identity of essence and existence.

The philosophers and theologians agree that the Necessary Being must be one. To prove this, they consider what would happen if there were two. This lesson walks you through the argument, which uses a disjunctive syllogism to eliminate all possibilities except unity.

Guided Path
1
Set up the hypothesis
Suppose there are two beings that are necessary in existence. They share the property of 'necessity of existence' but must differ by something, otherwise they would be identical.
2
Identify the disjunction
The differentiating factor is either the full reality (tamam al-haqiqa) of each or something other than the full reality.
This is an exclusive and exhaustive disjunction: both cannot be true and one must be true.
3
Analyze the first option
If distinction is by the full reality, then the shared property (necessity of existence) would be external to each essence. This contradicts the established fact that necessity is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being.
4
Analyze the second option
If distinction is not by the full reality, then it must be either a part of the essence or an accident. A part would make the essence composite, and an accident would make the essence dependent on another—both impossible for the Necessary Being.
5
Draw the conclusion
Both disjuncts lead to impossibilities, so the hypothesis that there are two necessary beings is false. Therefore, only one Necessary Being exists.
This argument is accepted by philosophers and theologians alike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في توحيد واجب الوجود. لأنا لو فرضنا موجودين واجبي الوجود لكانا مشتركين في وجوب الوجود متمايزين بأمر من الأمور، وما به الامتياز إما أن يكون تمام الحقيقة أو لا يكون، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن الامتياز لو كان بتمام الحقيقة لكان وجوب الوجود لاشتراكه خارجا عن حقيقة كل واحد منهما، وهو محال؛ لما ثبت من قبل أن وجوب الوجود عين حقيقة الواجب لذاته. وأما الثاني: فهو محال أيضا؛ لأن ما به الامتياز إن لم يكن تمام الحقيقة كان جزءا من الحقيقة أو عرضا، والأول يوجب التركيب في الحقيقة، والثاني يقتضي افتقار الواجب في ذاته إلى الغير، وكلاهما محال. وقد تبين أن مفهوم واجب الوجود لا يصدق في الخارج إلا على ذات واحدة، ويمتنع أن يصدق على غيرها. وهذه المسألة متفق عليها بين الفلاسفة والمتكلمين، لا يخالف فيها إلا الثنوية - خذلهم الله تعالى.
Section on the unity of the Necessary Being. For if we suppose two beings that are necessary in existence, they would share in the necessity of existence and be distinguished by something. That by which they are distinguished is either the full reality or not. The first possibility is not possible because if distinction were by the full reality, then the necessity of existence, due to it being shared, would be external to the reality of each—and that is impossible because it has been established previously that the necessity of existence is identical to the reality of the necessary in itself. As for the second, it is also impossible because if that by which they are distinguished is not the full reality, then it would be either a part of the reality or an accident. The first would entail composition in the reality, and the second would entail the necessary being in need of another in its essence—both are impossible. It has become clear that the concept of the necessary in existence is not true of anything in the external world except one single essence, and it is impossible for it to be true of another. This issue is agreed upon between philosophers and theologians; only the dualists (may Allah disgrace them) disagree.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the classic philosophical proof for the oneness of God (tawhid) used by the Peripatetic philosophers. The argument proceeds by considering a hypothetical scenario of two Necessary Beings. They would share the attribute of wujub al-wujud (necessity of existence), but since they are two, they must differ in some aspect. The differentiating factor is examined in two cases. First, if it is the full essence (tamam al-haqiqa), then wujub al-wujud would be external to each essence, contradicting the earlier proof that wujub is identical to the essence. Second, if it is not the full essence, it must be either a part (making the essence composite) or an accident (making the essence dependent), both of which are impossible for the Necessary Being. Thus, the hypothesis of two is absurd, proving that only one Necessary Being can exist. The author notes that this conclusion is accepted by both philosophers and theologians, with the exception of the dualists.

Key Terms
توحيد (tawhid): Unity; the doctrine that God is one. [Note: Standard theological term.]
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud): Necessary Being; that which necessarily exists by itself. [Note: Central term in Islamic metaphysics.]
تمام الحقيقة (tamam al-haqiqa): Full reality; the entire essence that distinguishes a thing. [Note: Used to indicate the complete quiddity.]
تركيب (tarkib): Composition; being made up of parts. [Note: In this context, composition is impossible for the Necessary Being.]
عرض (arad): Accident; a non-essential attribute. [Note: Standard definition.]
ثنوية (thanawiyya): Dualists; those who believe in two eternal principles, e.g., Zoroastrians. [Note: Referenced as opponents.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof for the unity of the Necessary Being, why is the first possibility (distinction by the full reality) impossible?
A) It would make the two beings indistinguishable.
B) It would require the necessity of existence to be external to each essence.
C) It would make each being composite.
D) It would lead to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive argument shows that any attempt to posit two necessary beings leads to an absurdity, confirming tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the disjunctive proof from the passage in your own words, explaining why each disjunct is impossible and how the conclusion follows.
?
Use the same disjunctive structure to prove that a certain act of worship (e.g., salah) is uniquely obligatory by showing that any alternative would lead to a contradiction in the nature of obligation.

Defining Azaliyya and Abadiyya: Eternity and Perpetuity
Author defines the technical meanings of أزلية and أبدية.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define azaliyya and abadiyya and distinguish them based on their temporal direction (past vs. future).

Core Idea
Azaliyya is beginningless past; abadiyya is endless future.

In Islamic philosophy, two key terms describe the timeless nature of certain beings: azaliyya (أزلية) and abadiyya (أبدية). The passage from Hidayat al-Hikma gives concise definitions: azaliyya is existence continuing in the past without being preceded by non-existence, while abadiyya is existence continuing in the future without being overtaken by non-existence. These are not just synonyms for 'eternal' but specify distinct temporal aspects. Understanding this distinction is crucial for discussions about God, the intellects, and the soul in the Islamic philosophical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being defined
The passage defines أزلية (azaliyya) and أبدية (abadiyya). Notice that both are derived from roots indicating timelessness but with different temporal directions.
2
Extract the definition of azaliyya
Azaliyya is defined as 'استمرار الوجود في الجانب الماضي بحيث لا يكون مسبوقا بالعدم' – continuation of existence in the past aspect without being preceded by non-existence. This means no beginning.
The phrase 'لا يكون مسبوقا بالعدم' is key: it negates any prior non-existence.
3
Extract the definition of abadiyya
Abadiyya is defined as 'استمرار الوجود في الجانب المستقبل بحيث لا يلحقه العدم' – continuation of existence in the future aspect without non-existence overtaking it. This means no end.
The phrase 'لا يلحقه العدم' indicates that non-existence will never catch up.
4
Contrast the two definitions
Azaliyya concerns the past (māḍī), abadiyya concerns the future (mustaqbal). Both share the condition of being free from non-existence. Something that is both azali and abadi is eternal in both directions.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the Necessary Being (Wājib al-Wujūd). It is described as both azali and abadi because it has neither beginning nor end. The intellects (ʿuqūl) in the emanation scheme are also considered azali and abadi because they are necessary through another.
In falsafa, the intellects are eternal in both directions, but their existence depends on God.

Source Text

أزلية العقول وهو عبارة عن استمرار الوجود في الجانب الماضي بحيث لا يكون مسبوقا بالعدم أبديتها هو عبارة عن استمرار الوجود في الجانب المستقبل بحيث لا يلحقه العدم.
Eternity of intellects: it is the expression for the continuation of existence in the past aspect such that it is not preceded by non-existence. Its perpetuity: it is the expression for the continuation of existence in the future aspect such that non-existence does not overtake it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definitions of two key terms in Islamic philosophy: أزلية (azaliyya) and أبدية (abadiyya). أزلية refers to the absence of a beginning in the past, meaning the entity has always existed without being preceded by non-existence. أبدية refers to the absence of an end in the future, meaning the entity will never cease to exist. These definitions are foundational for discussions about God and the intellects in the Neoplatonic framework of Hidayat al-Hikma. The reader should note that these terms are used specifically for immaterial beings that are necessary or derived from the Necessary Being. For example, the intellects are considered eternal and perpetual because they are necessary through another.

أزلية
أبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of أبدية?
A) Existence that is preceded by non-existence
B) Continuation of existence in the past without beginning
C) Continuation of existence in the future without end
D) Existence that is necessary in itself

Key Takeaway
Azaliyya and abadiyya are precise technical terms for existence without beginning and without end, respectively, and are used to describe the temporal mode of immaterial beings in Islamic philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Identify whether the following statements correctly use azaliyya and abadiyya: 'The human soul is azali (has no beginning in time) but not abadi (will not endure forever).' Explain your reasoning using the definitions from the passage.
?
Task 2: In a debate on the eternity of the world, a theologian claims that the world is both azali and abadi. Based on the definitions, what would you need to check about the world's existence in the past and future to evaluate this claim?
